ABSTRACT

The Texas Department of Transportation (TxDOT) is responsible for providing safe, effective,
and efficient movement of people and goods. As improvements to the system are partially funded
by the Federal Highway Administration, they are subject to the requirements of Section 106 of
the National Historic Preservation Act (of 1966, as amended) and the procedures for compliance
outlined in the implementing regulations 36 CFR 800. In conformity with the provisions of 36
CFR 800.14, compliance with the National Historic Preservation Act is conducted under the First
Amended Programmatic Agreement (PA-TU) among the Federal Highway Administration,
TxDOT, the Texas State Historical Preservation Officer, and the Advisory Council on Historic
Preservation Regarding the Implementation of Transportation Undertakings. TxDOT is also
subject to the Antiquities Code of Texas and its implementing regulations 13 TAC 26.
Procedures for compliance follow those outlined in the Memorandum of Understanding (MOU)
between the TxDOT and the Texas Historic Commission (13 TAC 26.14 and 43 TAC 2.24).
In order to partially fulfill its responsibilities under the National Historic Preservation Act and the
Antiquities Code of Texas, TxDOT contracted with Geo-Marine, Inc., under TxDOT Contract #
578 xx SA001, to conduct various archeological Surveys, Impact Evaluations, and Post-Review
Discovery investigations within the Atlanta, Dallas, Lufkin, Paris, and Tyler districts. The
fieldwork was conducted between June 2008 and June 2010 under Texas Antiquities Permit 4815.
Individual interim reports were prepared for each project and appropriate compliance procedures
were followed. This final report presents a compilation of all the individual interim reports.
Work authorizations issued under this contract were undertaken in three of the five districts: the
Dallas and Paris districts in Northcentral Texas; and the Atlanta, District in Northeast Texas. The
Dallas District consists of seven counties (Dallas, Denton, Collin, Ellis, Kaufman, Navarro, and
Rockwall). The Paris District covers a nine-county area (Delta, Fannin, Franklin, Grayson,
Hopkins, Hunt, Lamar, Rains, and Red River). The Atlanta District is also composed of a ninecounty area (Bowie, Camp, Cass, Harrison, Marion, Morris, Panola, Titus, and Upshur). The
construction projects undertaken in TxDOT districts focused on the replacement or rehabilitation
of specific bridge and relief structures on federal and state highways, farm-to-market roads, and
county roads. Other transportation projects involved road widening, post-review discovery
surveys, utility and road realignments, and construction of temporary haul roads. The 15 work
authorizations completed under this contract involved 33 surveys and two impact evaluations.
iii

The final work authorization also included the development and preparation of this report.
TxDOT defines impact evaluation as the initial step in determining whether subsequent survey of
a particular area is warranted, given the anticipated impacts of the project, the existing level of
disturbance, and the likelihood of archeological deposits in good context. Survey is defined by
TxDOT as locating all archeological remains through pedestrian surface examination, shovel
testing, and subsurface mechanical trenching (where appropriate) within the area of potential
effects. Post-review discovery survey consists of a site visit to rapidly evaluate potential historic
properties discovered during construction.
Fieldwork carried out under the various work authorizations investigated 14 sites plotted within
or extending into the project areas of potential effects: four previously recorded sites and 10
newly recorded sites. Of the four previously recorded sites (all prehistoric lithic scatters),
41HS13 was adjacent to the project area, but no indication of the site could be found within the
project area of potential effects. Sites 41BW166 and 41TT108 were found to extend within the
project areas. Site 41HS559 could not be fully investigated because access to the proposed new
right-of-way on which the site is plotted had not been granted at the time of survey, and thus
investigations were confined to the adjacent state-owned portion, where no cultural materials
were found.
Of the 10 newly recorded sites (all of which most likely extend beyond the project APEs), sites
41BW761 and 41BW766 were prehistoric artifact scatters, and 41BW769 and 41HS915 were
lithic scatters with minimal historic artifacts. Sites 41BW767 and 41DT274 (both with minimal
prehistoric artifacts), as well as historic sites 41BW768, 41DT272, 41DT273, and 41DT275
likely represent the remnants of early to mid-twentieth-century occupations.
Sites 41BW166 and 41BW769 were recommended for additional investigation, but the remaining
sites were recommended for no further work within the areas of potential effects. However, if
future impacts were to extend beyond the investigated portions of sites 41HS13, 41HS915, and
41TT108, monitoring or limited testing was recommended for these sites. Because access to site
41HS559 had not been granted at the time of survey, this site was not fully investigated, and
additional investigation was recommended when right-of-entry could be obtained.
All materials collected from these investigations on state property, including photographs, notes,
forms, and artifacts, will be permanently housed at the Texas Archeological Research Laboratory,
University of Texas at Austin, at the conclusion of this contract.

CHAPTER 1
INTRODUCTION

This document constitutes the final report for work done by Geo-Marine, Inc. (Geo-Marine,
Project #22001.00.20.06), under Contract No. 578 xx SA001 with the Texas Department of
Transportation (TxDOT) to provide archeological services in five TxDOT districts—Atlanta,
Dallas, Lufkin, Paris, and Tyler—in Northeast and Northcentral Texas (Figure 1). The contract
began in January 2008 and will conclude in December 2010. During this time, 15 work
authorizations were issued, with the final one being issued so that the draft version of this report
could be submitted in time for review and revisions prior to the end of the contract.
Under this contract, Geo-Marine completed impact evaluations and archeological surveys
(including two post-review discovery surveys) to assist TxDOT in meeting the requirements of its
Memorandum of Understanding (13 TAC 26.14 and 43 TAC 2.24) with the Texas Historical
Commission (THC) and the First Amended Programmatic Agreement (PA-TU) among the
Federal Highway Administration, the Texas Department of Transportation, the Texas State
Historical Preservation Officer, and the Advisory Council on Historic Preservation Regarding the
Implementation of Transportation Undertakings. TxDOT defines impact evaluation as a
generalized physical inspection of the project area to evaluate the potential for the project area to
contain sites with sufficient integrity to address important research questions (through minimal
shovel testing to examine the character of deposits) and to document any historic markers in the
vicinity of the project area. Thus, an impact evaluation is the initial field step in determining
whether subsequent survey of a particular area is warranted, given the anticipated impacts of the
transportation project, the existing level of disturbance, and the likelihood of archeological
deposits in good context. Subsequent to the impact evaluation, intensive archeological survey as
defined by TxDOT is archeological fieldwork that augments the impact evaluation with
subsurface examination to locate all archeological remains through shovel testing, handexcavated units, and/or mechanical trenching, where appropriate, within the area of potential
impact. Post-review discovery survey consists of a site visit to rapidly evaluate potential historic
properties discovered during construction.
Geographically, the five districts are in Northcentral (the Dallas and Paris districts) and Northeast
Texas (the Atlanta, Lufkin, and Tyler districts). The Dallas District is composed of seven
counties (Dallas, Denton, Collin, Ellis, Kaufman, Navarro, and Rockwall), and the Paris District
covers a nine-county area (Delta, Fannin, Franklin, Grayson, Hopkins, Hunt, Lamar, Rains, and
1

Red River). In the geographic Northeast Texas region, the Atlanta District covers a nine-county
area (Bowie, Camp, Cass, Harrison, Marion, Morris, Panola, Titus, and Upshur). The Lufkin
District also encompasses nine counties (Angelina, Houston, Nacogdoches, Polk, Sabine, San
Augustine, San Jacinto, Shelby, and Trinity), and the Tyler District consists of eight counties
(Anderson, Cherokee, Gregg, Henderson, Rusk, Smith, Van Zandt, and Wood).
The current contract involved 15 archeological work authorizations for 35 CSJs, involving field
projects in three (the Atlanta, Dallas, and Paris) of the five TxDOT districts. Some of the work
authorizations consisted of multiple tasks involving 33 archeological surveys and two impact
evaluations. The final work authorization additionally included the production of this report (five
work authorization numbers were not issued). Fieldwork was conducted between June 2008 and
June 2010 by Geo-Marine archeologists and architectural historians.
Most of the TxDOT construction projects focused on the replacement or rehabilitation of specific
bridges and relief structures on interstate or federal highways (IH or US) and state highways
(SH), farm-to-market (FM) roads, and county roads (CR). Other kinds of transportation activities
included road widenings, new temporary haul road constructions, and utility and road
realignments.
During the course of these investigations, 14 archeological site locations were either revisited
(41BW166, 41HS13, 41HS559, 41TT108) or newly recorded (41BW761, 41BW766, 41BW767,
41BW768, 41BW769, 41DT272, 41DT273, 41DT274, 41DT275, 41HS915). Among these 14
sites or portions of sites identified within the project areas, eight are prehistoric or predominantly
prehistoric (41BW761, 41BW766, 41HS13, 41BW166, 41BW769, 41HS915, and 41TT108), and
six are historic-era or predominantly historic-era (41BW768, 41DT272, 41DT275, 41BW767,
41DT273, and 41DT274) occupations. All collected artifacts were recovered within state-owned
right-of-way (ROW), and along with all materials generated by this project, will be permanently
curated at the Texas Archeology Research Laboratory (TARL) at the University of Texas at
Austin at the conclusion of this contract.
The body of this report presents a brief characterization of the environmental setting of the five
TxDOT districts; a synopsis of the cultural background of Northeast and Northcentral Texas;
summaries of impact evaluations and surveys conducted under this contract; discussions of the
site investigations and recommendations for National Register of Historic Places (NRHP)
eligibility and for State Archeological Landmark (SAL) designation; and the overall
recommendations concerning the impact evaluation and archeological survey results. The body
of the report is followed by a References Cited section and Appendix A, Glossary of Technical
Terms.

3

CHAPTER 2
ENVIRONMENTAL SETTING

The five TxDOT districts under this contract lie within the Blackland Prairie and Gulf Coastal
Plains physiographic provinces, which together form part of the Mesozoic-Cenozoic coastal
geosyncline (Murray 1960). This geosyncline consists of a gradually sloping basin that dips
toward the Gulf of Mexico and contains formations of limestone and sandstone deposited along
the margins of an ancient receding coastline. As the Gulf of Mexico formed during the late
Cretaceous, clastic sediments and carbonates were deposited over the worn-down Ouachita
Mountains to the northwest, and along the broad marginal shelf of the gulf basin. The Lower
Cretaceous sandstones, mudstones, limestones, and marls found throughout parts of the study
area represent cycles of marine transgression and regression throughout the period. By Upper
Cretaceous times, the gulf basin infilling and shoreline progradation predominated as evidenced
by Upper Cretaceous sandstones and mudstones throughout much of the region. The geologic
formations found in these areas are the Navarro and Taylor groups, Claiborne group, Wilcox and
Midway groups, and the Catahoula Formation, along with the ubiquitous Quaternary and
Holocene Alluvial sediments (Bureau of Economic Geology [BEG] 1992).
Within the five TxDOT districts under consideration in this study are six major drainage basins:
the Red River, the Sulphur River, Cypress Creek, the Sabine River, the Angelina/Neches rivers,
and the Trinity River (Figure 2). Four of these are among the 12 largest in the state of Texas.
The Red River, which flows east along the northern edge of the research area (in particular
forming the northern border of the Paris District), drains over 30,000 square miles in Texas (BEG
1996). The Sulphur River and Cypress Creek are considered part of the Red River macrodrainage. The Sabine River drains over 7,400 square miles over its 360-mile course and includes
two major reservoirs (BEG 1996). The Neches River runs for 416 miles and drains over 10,000
square miles of eastern Texas; the Angelina River is considered a tributary of the Neches, and
their confluence lies just south of the TxDOT Lufkin District (BEG 1996). Skirting the western
edge of the study area, the Trinity River basin is 550 miles long, drains over 17,000 square miles
of land, and includes 14 major reservoirs along its three major branches (BEG 1996).
The project areas investigated under this contract contain very diverse soil characteristics. The
macro divisions of environments include upland, floodplain, and terrace deposits. The upland
deposits vary from loams and sands overlaying clays or loams to all clay deposits. These areas
are gently sloping to moderately steep with varying degrees of permeability and drainage. The
5

ground cover in upland areas usually consists of woodland or pasture grasses; many of the upland
areas have suffered from erosion due to extensive timber cutting and poor management of soils.
Most upland soils are acidic in nature with some alkaline exceptions. The alluvial nature of
floodplains has made them highly variable in their pedological expressions. In high-energy
depositional environments, soils often consist entirely of sands, versus low-energy back swamps
containing fine clays. Large variations in deposition, including natural levees and point bars
contained in floodplains, were found in relatively small spatial areas. Ground cover in these
lowland areas varies from woodland and pasture to crops. These soils vary from poorly to
moderately well drained, and pH levels range from acidic to alkaline. Terrace deposits have
similar stratigraphy to some upland deposits, in addition to containing wind-modified deposits in
some areas. These areas are usually well drained, so much so that they often show evidence of
erosion such as gullies and deflation to the underlying geologic formations. The soils of
Northcentral and Northeast Texas are exemplified by broad diversity, and this can be a
challenging factor for archeologists to consider in their interpretations in impact evaluations and
archeological surveys (Fox 1980; Roberts 1983, 1990).
Climatically, the project areas are characterized by long hot summers and short cool winters.
Cold waves in the winter are rare and not severe, usually lasting only one or two days.
Temperatures rarely exceed 41 degrees Celsius (°C; 106° Fahrenheit [F]) in the summer, or fall
below -12°C (10°F) in the winter, but the variation is sufficient to produce four distinct seasons—
a mild winter, a warm spring, a hot summer, and a warm autumn. The number of days in the
growing season with temperatures above freezing averages between 228 and 270 (Fox 1980:2,
81; Nixon 1985:7; Roberts 1983, 1990).
Precipitation is fairly heavy throughout the year. Prolonged droughts are rare, and frequent
afternoon thunderstorms in summer are adequate to maintain crops. Severe storms, including
tornadoes, strike the area occasionally and often cause flooding and erosion. Every few years in
the summer or fall, a tropical depression moves inland, causing extremely heavy rains for one to
three days. Average annual precipitation ranges from 89 centimeters (cm) to over 127 cm (35–50
inches [in]) and is usually distributed quite uniformly throughout the year (Nixon 1985:7).
Snowfall is variable but rare (Fox 1980:2; Roberts 1983, 1990).
The modern plant communities vary from subregion to subregion (Figure 3). The Mixed PineHardwood Forest (forests having greater than 60 percent canopy) is composed of loblolly pine
(Pinus taeda), shortleaf pine (P. echinata), overcup oak (Quercus lyrata), swamp chestnut oak
(Q. michauxii), willow oak (Q. phellos), water oak (Q. nigra), sweetgum (Liquidambar
styraciflua), and sweetbay (Magnolia virginiana) (Diamond et al. 1987). Deciduous woodlands
(woodlands having 26–60 percent canopy) of bluejack oak (Q. incana; also known as sandjack
oak) and pine and swamp communities of bald cypress (Taxodium distichum), buttonbush
(Cephalanthus occidentalis), and water elm (Planera aquatica) are also a part of the Mixed PineHardwood Forest. The Oak Woodlands (i.e., Shortleaf Pine-Post Oak-Southern Red Oak) forest
covers portions of Northeast Texas. The most common trees present within this vegetation zone
are red oak (Q. rubra), post oak (Q. stellata), hickory (Carya Nutt.), and shortleaf pine (Roberts
1983, 1990:52). Other associated species within this vegetation zone are loblolly pine, black
hickory (Carya texana Buckley), sandjack oak, flowering dogwood (Cornus florida), common
persimmon (Diospyros virginiana), sweetgum, sassafras (Sassafras albidum), greenbrier (Smilax
spp.), yaupon (Ilex vomitoria), wax myrtle (Myrica cerifera), American beautyberry (Callicarpa
americana), hawthorn (Crataegus spp.), supplejack (Flagellaria indica), winged elm (Ulmus
alata), beaked panicum (Panicum anceps), spranglegrass (Diplachne spp.), Indiangrass
7

(Sorghastrum nutans), switchgrass (Panicum virgatum), three-awn (Aristida adscensionis),
bushclover (Lespedeza capitata), and tickclover (Desmodium illinoense) (McMahan et al.
1984:25). Although the Longleaf Pine Forest once covered millions of acres across the
southeastern United States, few stands of the forest are extant. Most of the old-growth forest has
been logged for timber and the original forest replaced with faster-growth pine species. However,
one old-growth Longleaf Pine Forest in Texas has been set aside for preservation in the southern
part of the Lufkin District. The old-growth forest of towering longleaf pine (Pinus palustris) has,
among other species, an understory of dogwood (Cornus spp.), blackjack oak (Q. marilandica),
and pawpaw (Asimina spp.) trees, with groundcover of various tall grasses (e.g., bluestem
[Andropogon spp.] and panicum [Panicum spp.]) and, in sandy barrens, prickly pear (Opuntia
spp.) and trailing phlox (Phlox nivalis) (Conservation Priorities for Texas 2008; McMahan et al.
1984; Natural Resources Conservation Service [NRCS] 2008).
The Blackland Prairie consists of tall grasslands, primarily little bluestem (Schizachyrium
scoparium) and Indiangrass (Sorghastrum nutans), with riparian deciduous forests of hackberry
(Celtis occidentalis), sugarberry (Celtis laevigata), and elm (Ulmus spp.) (Diamond et al. 1987).
The flora of the Eastern Cross Timbers is made up of deciduous forests of post oak (Q. stellata)
and black hickory (Carya texana), and woodlands of post oak and blackjack oak (Q.
marilandica). The Grand Prairie is a mixed-grass prairie dominated by little bluestem and
containing isolated live oak mottes as well as juniper (Juniperus spp.) and mesquite (Prosopis
spp.) savannas (Hayward et al. 1996). The Western Cross Timbers consists primarily of
deciduous woodlands of post oaks and blackjack oaks (Diamond et al. 1987).
In addition to the timber and wood resources of the area, acorns, nuts, berries, and grasses were
available to both prehistoric and historic-age groups. Trees bearing edible nuts identified as
being present in Northeast and Northcentral Texas include eastern black walnut (Juglans nigra),
pecan (Carya illinoinensis), shellbark hickory (C. laciniosa), shagbark hickory (C. ovata),
mockernut hickory (C. alba), black hickory (C. texana), nutmeg hickory (C. myristiciformis),
swamp hickory (C. cordiformus), pignut hickory (C. glabra), American beech (Fagus
grandifolia), Allegheny chinquapin (Castanea pumila), Ashe chinquapin (Castanea pumila
ozarkensis), white oak (Q. alba), blackjack oak (Q. marilandica), and live oak (Q. virginiana)
(Heartfield and Dieste 1984a, 1984b; Vines 1977).
Faunal resources available to prehistoric and historic-age populations in Northeast and
Northcentral Texas would have included numerous types of invertebrates along the major
streams, as well as various species of mollusks, both bivalves and gastropods, in addition to
crustaceans (Swanton 1946). In prehistoric and early historic times, fish resources would have
included gar (Lepisosteus spp.), crappie (Poxomis spp.), white bass (Morone spp.), black bass
(Micropterus spp.), buffalo (Ictiomus spp.), shad (Dorosoma spp.), bowfin (Amia calva), various
shiners (Notropis, Cyprinella, and Notemigonus spp.), chain pickerel (Esox niger), catfish
(Ictalurus and Pylodictus spp.), sunfish (Lepomis spp.), and freshwater drum (Aplodinotus
grunniens) (Blair 1950; Heartfield and Dieste 1984a, 1984b:2–6). Various species of frogs,
turtles, and salamanders, as well as alligators (Alligator sinensis) would have been present.
A wide variety of migratory birds such as ducks, geese, and cranes (families Anatidae and
Gruidae would have been most numerous in the late fall and early winter, and other resident
birds, such as turkeys (Meleagris gallopavo) and mourning doves (Zenaida macroura), would
have been available year-round. Mammal species would have consisted of whitetail deer
(Odocoileus virginianus), bear (Ursus americanus), squirrel (Sciurus spp.), rabbit (Sylvilagus
9

spp.), opossum (Didelphis marsupialis), and raccoon (Procyon lotor) (Swanton 1946). Valuable
fur-bearing animals would have included rabbit, beaver (Castor canadensis), raccoon, weasel and
mink (Mustela spp.), ringtail (Bassariscus astutus), opossum, red fox (Vulpes vulpes), gray fox
(Urocyon cinereoargenteus), bobcat (Felis rufus), coyote (Canis latrans), badger (Taxidea taxus),
spotted skunk (Spilogale spp.), striped skunk (Mephitis mephitis), muskrat (Ondatra zibethicus),
and river otter (Lutra canadensis) (Blair 1950). Several economically important species that may
have once inhabited the area, such as pronghorn antelope (Antilocapra americana) and bison
(Bison bison), are now locally extinct (Blair 1950:100–102).

10

CHAPTER 3
CULTURAL SETTING

INTRODUCTION
The following chapter is intended to provide a general background to the archeology of both
Northeast and Northcentral Texas, while supplying the interested reader with sufficient references
for a more in-depth study of specific cultural periods. This chapter is divided into three sections,
the first of which gives a brief summary of previous archeological investigations in the research
areas; given the size of the region, this section must necessarily be limited in scope. The second
section briefly discusses the nature of the prehistoric and historic-age Native American
archeological records in broad terms, and the third considers the historic-age European and
American settlement in the area.

PREVIOUS ARCHEOLOGICAL INVESTIGATIONS
Early archeological research in Northcentral and Northeast Texas was intermittent and of limited
scope. The earliest known investigations in Northeast Texas occurred in 1879–1880 and were
conducted by Edward Palmer of the Peabody Museum. Although his primary occupation was the
collection of plants, he also collected artifacts for the museum and wished to ascertain whether
the ―Mound Builders‖ were to be found in East Texas. His investigation was apparently cursory,
as Palmer concluded that mound sites were not to be found in Texas (McVaugh 1956; Putnam
1880). After this, no archeology is known to have been undertaken in the northeastern portion of
Texas until early in the twentieth century, when Clarence B. Moore of the Philadelphia Academy
of Science ascended the Red River to investigate the archeology of Louisiana, Arkansas, and
Texas, publishing his findings in 1912. No excavations were undertaken in Texas, but the Cabe
Mounds site (41BW14), the E. H. Moores site (41BW2), and the A. J. Hatchel site (41BW3) were
examined (Moore 1912).
Beginning in the 1920s, more intensive and regionally based archeological investigations were
begun by J. E. Pearce, the founder of the Department of Anthropology at the University of Texas.
Early research was concentrated in Northeast Texas due to the large number of mound and burial
sites reported for this region. In 1919, Pearce was able to get funding from the Bureau of
American Ethnology (BAE) that allowed a reconnaissance into Northeast Texas. Based on the
11

examination of numerous collections, visits to sites in 14 counties, and limited test excavations,
Pearce concluded that Northeast Texas had tremendous research potential (Barnard 1939; Pearce
1924, 1931, 1932a). Funding proved to be the major constraint on Pearce‘s research, as he did
not receive another major grant until 1927, when he was awarded monies from the Laura
Spelman Rockefeller Foundation of New York City (Barnard 1939). From 1920 until 1935,
Pearce and his crews conducted excavations at approximately 123 sites in Northeast Texas.
Gardner (1931), Goldschmidt (1935a, 1935b), Jackson (1933, 1934, 1935, 1936, 1938), and
Pearce (1931, 1932a, 1932b) reported on some of those investigations.
Truly intensive archeological research had to wait until the Depression, when archeological
projects became one component of the Works Progress Administration (WPA), a federal jobs
program. Funding under the WPA greatly exceeded anything previously seen in Texas, allowing
extensive site investigations and the accumulation of a huge regional database. Among the sites
investigated by the WPA in Northeast Texas were the Hatchel-Mitchell site complex (41BW3
and 41BW4, respectively) along the Red River (Davis 1970; Doran 1975; Dow 1987; Duffen et
al. 1940; Gilmore and McCormick 1980; Krieger 1946; Maples 1962; Suhm et al. 1954); the
George C. Davis site (41CE19) along the Neches River (Duffen et al. 1940; Ekland-Olson 1981;
Fritz 1975; Keller 1974; Newell and Krieger 1949; Shafer 1973; Spock 1977); and the Yarbrough
(41VN6) and Joslin (41VN3) sites in the Sabine River basin (Johnson 1962). Only interim
reports were published for much of that work; however, the database thus derived has provided
fertile ground for later students.
Avocational archeologists from Dallas, Texarkana, and elsewhere also began to work intensively
in the region during the 1930s (Harris 1964; Miroir n.d.). The most prominent was Dr. Clarence
H. Webb, a pediatrician from Shreveport, Louisiana. Webb‘s work in northern Louisiana and
East Texas was influential in the development of the regional culture history, especially for the
Caddoan tradition (e.g., Ford and Webb 1956; Fulton and Webb 1953; Webb 1941, 1945, 1946,
1948a, 1948b, 1956, 1959, 1960, 1961, 1963, 1968; Webb and Dodd 1939, 1941; Webb and
McKinney 1975; Webb et al. 1969, 1971). Some of the sites excavated by Webb and his
associates include the Smithport Landing site (16DS4), the Belcher Mound site (16CD13), the
Gahagan Mound site (16RR1), the Mounds Plantation (16CD12), and the Resch site (41HS16).
At the same time, nonprofessionals and collectors from Dallas and Texarkana also began to
excavate into Caddoan mound and cemetery sites in extreme Northeast Texas (see, for example,
Harris 1953 and Miroir n.d.).
After World War II, there was no need for the WPA, and large-scale investigations were no
longer funded by the federal government. However, since the government was proposing the
construction of a series of reservoirs across the country, it was determined that archeological
investigations would be funded in order to salvage some of the data that would otherwise be
destroyed. The National Park Service served as the lead agency for this work, but the actual
fieldwork was undertaken by the River Basin Survey (RBS), which was formed as a part of the
Bureau of American Ethnology. Investigations undertaken at relevant lakes in North Texas by
the RBS and successor organizations included Lake O‘ the Pines (Davis 1961; Davis 1958, 1970;
Davis and Gipson 1960; Davis and Golden 1960; Jelks and Tunnell 1959; Lisk 1984; Miller et al.
1951; Thurmond 1981, 1990; Tunnell 1959; Turner 1978); Wright Patman Lake (Briggs and
Malone 1970; Jelks 1961; Stephenson 1950); and Lake Tawakoni (Duffield 1961; Duffield and
Jelks 1961; Johnson 1957). Other regionally important examples include Cooper Lake (Duffield
1959; Gilmore and Hoffrichter 1964; Harris 1955; Hatzenbuehler 1953; Johnson 1962; Moorman
and Jelks 1952) along the Sulphur River; and Lake Lavon (Dawson and Sullivan 1973; Lynott
1975; Stephenson 1949, 1952) and Lake Ray Hubbard (Harris and Suhm 1963; Lorrain and
12

Hoffrichter 1968) along the East Fork of the Trinity River. Much of the excavation effort within
the upper Trinity River basin has similarly focused on reservoir development, especially along the
Elm Fork (Crook and Harris 1957, 1958, 1961; Skinner and Baird 1985; Skinner et al. 1982) and
the East Fork (Dawson and Sullivan 1973; Lorrain and Hoffrichter 1968; Lynott 1975; Ross
1966).
Beginning in the late 1960s, federal and state governments began to require consideration of the
potential environmental impacts of government-funded projects. These regulations included
assessing the impacts to archeological resources. During the last several decades, the gradual
growth of the cultural resources management (CRM) industry, composed of government
agencies, universities, and private companies, has taken place. Survey and testing were
undertaken at a number of Northcentral and Northeast Texas reservoirs during this period: Lake
Bob Sandlin (Driggers 1985; Flaigg 1982; Herrington 1979; Hsu 1969; Sullivan 1975; Thurmond
1981, 1990); Lake Fork Reservoir (Bruseth 1975; Bruseth and Perttula 1981; Bruseth et al. 1977;
Crane 1982; Perttula and Bruseth 1983; Skiles et al. 1980; Skinner 1975a; Thurmond 1985);
Black Cypress Reservoir (Northern and Skiles 1981); Big Sandy Reservoir (Gibson 1982;
Perttula et al. 1986); and Lake Gilmer (Horizon Environmental Services, Inc., 1992; Nichols et al.
1997). In addition, further investigations were undertaken at previously inundated lakes such as
Lake O‘ the Pines (Gulf South Research Institute 1974; Thurmond 1981, 1990), Wright Patman
Lake (Shiner 1982), and Caddo Lake (Campbell et al. 1983; Gibson 1969; Gulf South Research
Institute 1974; Thurmond 1981, 1990). In the late 1990s and into the 2000s, reservoir surveys
were undertaken at Toledo Bend for the U.S. Army Corps of Engineers (USACE; Drake 2000),
Texas Parks and Wildlife Department (Robinson 1997), and the U.S. Forest Service (Skiles
1993); at Lake Livingston (Driver 2004; Galan 2000) for private corporations; and at Lake Sam
Rayburn (Jones and Trierweiler 2005; Nichols 1995; Trierweiler and Galan 2002) for the
USACE.
Intensive CRM studies have been undertaken at Longhorn Army Ammunition Plant in eastern
Harrison County, near Karnack (Cliff et al. 1995; Cliff and Peter 1994b; Cliff, Peter, Largent, and
Waite 1996; Dieste et al. 1985; Peter and Stiles-Hanson 1989). Several lignite mines have also
been examined in the area, including Karnack (Heartfield, Price, and Green, Inc., 1986), Darco
(Carlson 1985; Corbin et al. 1976; Perttula and Skiles 1987; Studer 1980, 1982), and South
Hallsville (Dibble 1977; Espey Huston & Associates, Inc., 1979, 1984; Freeman 1978; Keller
1990a, 1990b, 1992, 1993, 1994, 1996a, 1996b, 1998, 1999, 2000a, 2000b; LaVardera 1983a,
1983b, 1984a, 1984b, 1985; North American Consultants, Inc., 1984, 1985). More recent work in
the Caddo Lake area has focused on surveys of Harrison Bayou (Perttula and Nelson 1999), test
excavations at the Timber Hill site, a historic-age Caddo village on James Bayou (McCrocklin
1998; Parsons 1999), and intensive survey and some test excavations at Lake Bob Sandlin Park in
the Big Cypress Creek drainage (Perttula and Nelson 2002).
Two large, recent projects near Wright Patman Lake (formerly Lake Texarkana) have been
undertaken at the White Oak Creek Wildlife Management Area (WOCWMA) in Cass, Bowie,
Morris, and Titus counties, and at the Red River Army Depot and Lone Star Army Ammunition
Plant (RRAD/LSAAP) in Bowie County. The former was developed by the USACE to mitigate
the loss of wildlife habitat due to inundation of Cooper Lake farther upstream along the Sulphur
River. It consists of approximately 25,000 acres of bottomlands and the adjacent upland margins
along White Oak Creek and the Sulphur River. These investigations began with the preparation
of a research design to guide the work (Peter, Cliff, and Abbott 1990) and have included surveys
(Cliff 1994; Cliff and Peter 1992; Cliff, White, Hunt, Pleasant, and Shaw 1996), site testing (Cliff
and Hunt 1995; Largent et al. 1997), and data recovery (Cliff, Green, Hunt, Shanabrook, and
13

Peter 1996). The other project consisted of a series of studies designed to inventory and evaluate
the cultural resources present in the RRAD/LSAAP in central Bowie County. These
investigations have included surveys (Cliff and Peter 1994a; Cliff, Hunt, Green, Peter, and Kent
1996; Gadus and Freeman 1998; Newman 1988; Peter and Cliff 1990a, 1990b), limited testing
(Cliff and Peter 1988), and the preparation of a cultural resources management plan, including an
overview of Bowie County and the Great Bend in general (Peter et al. 1991:Appendix I).
To the west of Wright Patman Lake, in Titus and Franklin counties, extensive cultural resources
investigations have been undertaken by Espey Huston & Associates, Inc. in association with
surface coal mining projects. At the Monticello B-2 Mine, north of Mount Pleasant, 237
prehistoric and historic-age archeological sites were recorded within approximately 20,000 acres
(Jones et al. 1993). Subsequently, testing was conducted at six prehistoric sites in 1991 (Kotter et
al. 1993) and an additional 10 sites in 1993 (Nash et al. 1995). On the basis of this work, data
recovery was conducted at two sites in 1993 and 1994 (Dixon et al. 1995, 1998; Perttula 1998a;
Perttula and Nelson 1995; Perttula, Neff, Glascock, Mulholland, Nelson, Tate, Cogswell, Skokan,
and Rogers 1998). Additional site testing in 1996 resulted in data recovery at the Ear Spool site
(41TT653), a Late Caddo Titus phase farmstead (Galan 1998). Elsewhere in Titus County, a
survey of 2,716 acres at the Monticello I Area, southwest of Mount Pleasant, recorded another 22
prehistoric and historic-age sites (Hoyt et al. 1994). TxDOT projects in Titus County include
data recovery at site 41TT13 (Rogers et al. 2003) as well as survey and ongoing testing along the
proposed relief route for US 271 around Mount Pleasant.
Other recent archeological activities of importance in Northeast Texas include the 1987
excavation by the Texas Department of Highways and Public Transportation of a small Caddoan
hamlet and cemetery at the Murphy Branch site (41MX5) north of White Oak Creek (Brewington
and Dockall 1991; Brewington et al. 1995; Wormser 1988); data recovery at site 41BW422 along
Barkman Creek north of Hooks in Bowie County (Tucker 1994); excavations at the Oak Hill
Village site (41RK214), a large Caddo village in Rusk County (Cruse 1995; Rogers and Perttula
2004); excavations at the Rookery Ridge site (41UR133), a Late Caddo hamlet at Lake Gilmer in
Upshur County (Parsons 1998); and data recovery at the Pilgrim‘s Pride site (41CP304), an
Archaic and Late Caddo Titus phase site in Camp County (Perttula 1999). Recent projects in the
immediate vicinity of Wright Patman Lake include a survey of the Beltex Lateral Pipeline in Cass
County south of the lake (Skinner 1992); small surveys conducted at Atlanta State Park for the
Texas Parks and Wildlife Department (Perttula 1998b; Perttula, Prikryl, and Nelson 1997; Turpin
1994a, 1994b); a number of surveys of timber-cutting areas surrounding the lake (Burson and
Cliff 2000; Cliff and Hunt 1998; Cliff, Hunt, Pleasant, Procter, and Ensor 1996; Hunt and
Borrego 2002; Hunt and Cliff 1998a, 1998b; Linder-Linsley and Lindsay 1997, 1998; Moore et
al. 1998; White et al. 1995); and testing of five prehistoric sites at the proposed Malden Lake
Park expansion (Owens and Hunt 1999). Recent investigations at Lake O‘ the Pines include
several timber-cutting-area surveys (Burson and Cliff 2000; Cliff and Hunt 1998; Hunt and
Borrego 2002; Hunt and Cliff 1998a, 1998b; Hunt et al. 1995; Linder-Linsley and Lindsay 1997,
1998; Moore et al. 1998), a survey of two proposed pump stations (Brazos Valley Research
Associates 1997), a city of Longview water pipeline (Perttula, Turner, and Nelson 1998), and
investigations by the Friends of Northeast Texas Archaeology archeologists (Perttula and Nelson
1997, 2001; Perttula et al. 1996). More recently, Geo-Marine completed an intensive pedestrian
survey of the new 51-kilometer (km; 32-mile [mi]) Lake O‘ the Pines South Side Regional Water
Supply System that traverses parts of Gregg, Harrison, Marion, and Upshur Counties (Largent,
Lintz, Huhnke, Hunt, Allday, Salo, and Green 2004), identifying nine new archeological sites and
revisiting two previously known sites.
14

The delay of the construction of Cooper Lake until the 1980s contributed to a more intensive
investigation of Cooper Lake than of other reservoirs. This work was conducted in three phases:
(1) the early investigations (1952–1964) mentioned above, (2) a return to Cooper Lake in the
1970s (Doehner and Larson 1978; Doehner et al. 1978; Hyatt and Doehner 1975; Hyatt and
Skinner 1971; Hyatt et al. 1974), and (3) mitigation efforts in the 1980s and early 1990s (Bailey
et al. 1991; Bousman et al. 1988; Cliff 1994; Fields et al. 1991; Fields et al. 1993; Fields and
Gardner 1991; Gadus et al. 1991; Gadus, Fields, and Bousman 1992; Gadus, Fields, Bousman,
and Howard 1992; Jurney and Bohlin 1993; Jurney et al. 1993; Lebo 1988; McGregor and
Roemer 1989; Moir and Jurney 1988; Moir et al. 1989; Perttula 1988a, 1988b, 1989a, 1989b;
Shepard et al. 1994; Winchell et al. 1992).
In Nacogdoches County, several recent projects have been completed in the Lake Naconiche area.
An archeological survey, recording 61 prehistoric and historic sites, was undertaken for the
proposed 1,265-acre floodwater and recreational structure on Naconiche Creek for Nacogdoches
County, sponsored by the Texas Natural Resource Conservation Commission (Perttula 2000).
Subsequently, 10 prehistoric sites were selected for Phase II testing based on their research
potential (Perttula 2002). More recently, intensive survey for a gas pipeline in the area was
undertaken to determine the presence/absence of cultural resources, particularly focusing on the
area where the line crossed Naconiche Creek (Iruegas 2004). Another project in Northeast Texas
involved data recovery at the Broadway site (41SM273), situated along a tributary of the
Angelina River in Smith County (Perttula and Nelson 2004). Additionally, Perttula (2006)
completed a study of cultural materials from numerous Northeast Texas Caddo sites held in a
private collection.
In Northcentral Texas, extensive archeological investigations have been undertaken as a result of
the construction of Lewisville Lake (formerly known as Lake Dallas and Garza-Little Elm
Reservoir) and Lake Ray Roberts (formerly known as Aubrey Reservoir). Lewisville Lake has
been expanded several times as the population of the Dallas-Fort Worth area has grown; its
earliest incarnation, Lake Dallas, existed prior to 1936. Archeological investigations for
Lewisville Lake began in 1948, when archeologists from the RBS investigated the proposed
reservoir area. Twenty-seven sites were found during this survey; 14 had previously been
recorded by members of the Dallas Archeological Society (DAS; Stephenson 1949, 1950). Three
sites, Ledbetter (41DN5), Lake Dallas (41DN6), and Bob Pierce (41DN12), were tested by RBS
crews in 1951, although the results have never been reported (Prikryl 1987). During and after the
RBS investigation, members of the DAS continued to investigate the Lewisville Lake area
(Barber 1966, 1969; Harris 1950, 1951a, 1951b). The major result of this work was the discovery
and investigation of the Lewisville Lake site (41DN72), a purported pre-Clovis occupation
(Crook 1964; Crook and Harris 1956, 1957, 1958, 1961). Radiocarbon dates of 37,000 years
before present (B.P.) from 41DN72 created considerable controversy in the archeological
community until 1979–1980, when lowered lake levels allowed reinvestigation of the site and
clarification of the dates by archeologists from the Smithsonian Institution (Stanford 1981).
Further enlargements of Lewisville Lake prompted surveys by the Richland Archeological
Society in 1973 (Nunley 1973) and archeologists from North Texas State University (now the
University of North Texas [UNT]) in 1986 (Lebo and Brown 1990). Test excavations and
mitigation (Ferring and Yates 1997; Lebo 1995a) of selected sites followed. An apparently
unpublished project undertaken by Southern Methodist University archeologists in 1975 resulted
in the recording of several sites near Lewisville Lake.

15

Archeological investigations at Lake Ray Roberts began in the early 1970s with a survey
conducted by crews from Southern Methodist University (Bousman and Verrett 1973) that
recorded 26 sites. During 1980–1981, additional survey, as well as testing and mitigation, was
undertaken by archeologists from Environmental Consultants, Inc. (ECI; Cliff et al. 1982), which
recorded 355 sites. Following the survey, additional investigations undertaken at 60 sites
(Skinner et al. 1982) were followed by intensive excavation at 12 sites, oral interviews for 17
historic-age sites, and architectural recording at 13 sites (Skinner and Baird 1985). Finally, in
1986, UNT crews began further survey, test excavations, and mitigation efforts within the area to
be affected by construction of Lake Ray Roberts (Ferring and Yates 1997; Lebo 1992, 1995b,
1996; Prikryl and Yates 1987). The Aubrey Clovis site (41DN479) was discovered
serendipitously in December 1988, when excavation of a channel connecting the new Lake Ray
Roberts spillway to the Elm Fork‘s natural channel produced Pleistocene faunal material in
association with Clovis artifacts (Ferring 1989). Recent investigations there have revealed
cultural deposits dated to 11,550 B.P., among the oldest known in North America (Ferring 2001).
The University of Texas at Arlington field school conducted excavations at the Northlake site on
Grapevine Creek in the mid-1970s (Morgan 1975). More recent investigations at Joe Pool Lake
(Jurney et al. 1988; Peter and McGregor 1988) and the test excavations of the River Bend site,
41TR68 (Peter et al. 1987), provided the initial assemblage data necessary for an understanding
of the adaptations along the West Fork of the Trinity River. In 1991 and 1992, the Texas
Archeological Society (TAS) conducted field school excavations under the direction of staff
members of the THC at several sites in the vicinity of the Sam Kaufman/Arnold Roitsch site
(41RR16) in Red River County, northwest of Wright Patman Lake (Bruseth 1992; Bruseth et al.
1991:1, 6–9; Martin 1992; Prikryl 1992; THC 1991:11–13). This research program included
excavations at the Fasken Mounds site (41RR14), the Ray site (41LR135), Salt Well Slough
(41RR204), and the Kaufman/Roitsch site itself. Also as part of this research effort, survey
operations were conducted within the Big Pine Creek drainage to the west, recording or
rerecording 109 sites (THC 1991:13).
In addition to the above projects, innumerable projects of varying scope have also been
completed in Northcentral Texas; the following account is not exhaustive by any means. In
Collin County a survey of the Clemons Creek watershed project sponsored by the Soil
Conservation Service (SCS) was conducted in 1974 (Hughston and Lynott 1974). In Hunt
County, two projects were sponsored by the SCS in the Lake Fork Creek watershed (Ferring and
Bagot 1974; Hyatt 1974; Skinner 1975b) and Pilot Grove Creek watershed (Lynott 1974;
McCormick and Filson 1977). Investigations initiated by the Texas Water Development Board
were completed for the cities of Renner (Whitsett 1977) and McKinney (Fox 1979) in the late
1970s. A survey of a proposed pipeline route across the northern section of Collin County was
completed in 1981 for Matador Pipelines, Inc. (Peter 1981). In 1984, a survey was sponsored by
the Farmers Electric Cooperative (Skinner 1984). Previous investigations for the North Texas
Municipal Water District (NTMWD) include pedestrian survey and backhoe trenching along the
western perimeter of the federal lands adjoining Lake Lavon, which resulted in the recording of
one prehistoric site (Peter, Cliff, Hunt, and Allday 1990), while similar investigations along the
eastern edge of Lake Lavon also resulted in the recording of one prehistoric site (Hunt et al.
1991). Martin (1988) conducted a survey of proposed highway improvements along IH 35W in
Denton and Tarrant counties. In 1989, UNT crews surveyed Hickory Creek Park for the USACE,
resulting in the location of one historic-age site (Lebo and Brown 1989). In 1991, Mariah
Associates, Inc., undertook a reconnaissance of proposed sewer line improvements for the city of
Argyle (Kibler 1991). During late 1991, Austin and Peter (1992) performed a pedestrian survey
16

of proposed improvements at North Lake Park in the city of Denton. Corbin (1992a) surveyed
the proposed Northpointe Park for the city of The Colony. Investigations concerning the
expansion of the city of McKinney solid waste landfill (Hunt et al. 1992) and a proposed water
pump station (Hunt 1993) have also been conducted for the NTMWD.
During 1993, AR Consultants surveyed the proposed 28-ac Highland Village Community Park
(Skinner 1993). In the same year, Peter and Green (1993) surveyed the proposed South Lakes
Park for the city of Denton. In 1994, Geo-Marine conducted a survey of the proposed SH 190
project area, identifying one new historic-age site (41DL366) and revisiting several others (Hunt
and Peter 1997). Ferring (1995) undertook a survey of 125 acres adjacent to the Lewisville Lake
dam. SWCA, Inc., conducted a pedestrian survey of approximately 8 acres belonging to the
USACE near Grapevine Lake (Miller 1995). Also in 1995, the University of Texas at Austin
conducted a survey of an 800-acre tract along Timber Creek (Carpenter 1995). During late 1994
and early 1995, Hicks & Company performed a pedestrian survey for a 160-acre expansion of the
city of Denton landfill (Treece et al. 1995). A 1996 pedestrian survey of the Arbor Hills Nature
Preserve by AR Consultants resulted in the location of one historic-age site (Skinner and Whorton
1996). Again in 1996, AR Consultants surveyed the Prairie Creek Interceptor for the city of
Lewisville (Trask et al. 1996). In the winter of 1996–1997, AR Consultants surveyed the
proposed FM 2499 at Lewisville Lake (Skinner 1997). Early in 1997, Espey Huston &
Associates, Inc., undertook survey of the Kirkwood Branch Interceptor for the Trinity River
Authority (Tate 1997). In 1998, AR Consultants conducted excavations at the Chinn Chapel
Cemetery Log Cabin, which, like the associated Chinn Chapel and Chinn Chapel Cemetery (both
of which are commemorated with State Historical markers), is located near Copper Canyon
(Skinner 1998). In 1999, Geo-Marine conducted further investigations along the route of the
proposed President George Bush Turnpike (SH 190), locating two previously recorded sites
(41DL240 and 41DL321) as well as a short-term prehistoric habitation site, 41DL391, whose data
potential was exhausted through test excavations (Peter et al. 2001, 2002).
The next year, a survey of a 109-acre reclamation area in the SH 190 corridor identified a
historic-age home site, 41DN394, just west of IH 35 (Hunt 2000); this site was determined to be
ineligible. The same year, AR Consultants identified sites 41DN510 and 41DN513, two historicage wells, in the proposed location of the planned community of Lantana (near Bartonville); the
report apparently remains unpublished. In 2001, Geo-Marine conducted archeological and
geoarcheological investigations of 15 acres along Timber Creek in Lewisville, identifying two
prehistoric sites considered ineligible for inclusion in the NRHP (Cooper et al. 2001). Site
41DN518, a historic-age farmstead, was reported by Galan in 2001. In 2002, Geo-Marine
conducted a survey of the proposed location for a new wastewater treatment facility at Panther
Creek, resulting in the identification of two historic-age sites (41DN519 and 41DN520); neither
was recommended for inclusion in the NRHP (Tiné 2002). In late 2002, the area immediately
northeast of the IH 35 crossing of the Elm Fork in Denton County was surveyed and subjected to
geoarcheological investigations by Geo-Marine, in preparation for the proposed T. C. Rice, Jr.,
Athletic Complex; no cultural materials were identified, with the exception of two small historicage localities (Hunt et al. 2002). In 2003, Geo-Marine conducted a survey of the linear 19.9-km
(11.9-mi) pipeline corridor located south of Denton. This survey, which crossed Hickory Creek
in numerous places, identified one prehistoric site and one historic-age locality. An Ellis-type
projectile point was recovered from the prehistoric site, which was located in a disturbed context
on an oil drill pad (Huhnke et al. 2003). Geo-Marine also surveyed a 20.3-km (12.2-mi) segment
of US 380 from the Denton/Wise county line to its intersection at IH 35 (Huhnke 2004). In
February of 2004, Geo-Marine surveyed a 51.5-km (32.2-mi) segment of IH 35 from IH 635 to
17

US 380 in Denton, resulting in the identification of three ineligible historic-age sites (Largent,
Huhnke, and Wurtz 2004). Later that year, Geo-Marine surveyed a 220-acre parcel on the
northeast corner of the intersection of IH 35 and the SH 121 bypass, locating the remains of two
previously recorded sites as well as one large, deeply buried Archaic site, 41DN529 (Largent,
Lintz, and Huhnke 2004).
Between 2000 and 2007, Geo-Marine completed 158 work authorizations under five TxDOT
contracts for the Atlanta, Dallas, Lufkin, Paris, and Tyler districts (Allday and Bastis 2008;
Largent et al. 2005; Phillips et al. 2007; Peukert et al. 2003; Wurtz et al. 2006). These work
authorizations consisted of multiple tasks involving primarily impact evaluations and
archeological surveys, along with several site testing projects, undertaken for various road and
bridge construction, widening, and rehabilitation projects. These investigations recorded or
revisited 105 sites along multiple federal, state, county, and farm-to-market roads and highways.
In addition to the work conducted by Geo-Marine, numerous other surveys have been conducted
in the Atlanta, Dallas, Lufkin, Paris, and Tyler districts. These surveys ranged in size from less
than an acre to more than 1,000 acres, resulting in the investigation of well over 100 sites (THC
2007).

NATIVE AMERICAN CULTURAL CHRONOLOGY
The following brief discussion of the prehistoric archeological record of Northeast Texas draws
from previous summaries by Kenmotsu and Perttula (1993), Perttula (1988a), Story (1981, 1985,
1990), and Thurmond (1985, 1988, 1990). Similarly, discussion of the archeology of
Northcentral Texas (especially for the upper Trinity River basin) derives from four major reports:
Peter and McGregor (1988), Prikryl (1987, 1990), and Yates and Ferring (1986). Although the
cultural sequences are similar, they are by no means identical, especially for Northeast Texas.
Indeed, due to what appears to be increasing regional differentiation through time, the cultural
sequences for the Great Bend Region (including Wright Patman Lake) and the western Cypress
Creek basin (including Lake O‘ the Pines) diverge in the Caddoan period. The generalized
cultural sequences for the Northcentral and Northeast Texas culture areas (Figure 4) are presented
in Table 1, and presented separately in the subsequent discussions.

Northeast Texas
This discussion concerning Northeast Texas applies to the Atlanta, Tyler, and Lufkin TxDOT
districts; the Northcentral Texas discussion applies to the Dallas and Paris districts. As indicated
in Table 1, the Native American cultural sequence for Northcentral Texas is quite different from
that of Northeast Texas. The only period of overlap appears to be the Paleo-Indian period, which
occurred before significant regional differentiation took place at the end of the Pleistocene; thus,
the Paleo-Indian discussion is equally applicable to Northeast and Northcentral Texas. Both
periods experienced a lengthy Archaic period as well, but it lasted much longer in Northcentral
Texas than in Northeast Texas, and the recognized cultures are not entirely the same.

Paleo-Indian Period
Although a pre-Clovis occupation of the New World had long been suggested (Adovasio et al.
1990; Dincauze 1984; Haynes et al. 1984; Kelly and Todd 1988; Lynch 1990; McGahey n.d.;
Meltzer 1989), it was not until the excavations at the Monte Verde site in Chile that the evidence
for a pre-12,000 B.P. occupation has been widely accepted. Within the southern and southeastern
United States, however, the evidence for a pre-Clovis occupation remains disputed, and thus the
Paleo-Indian period remains the earliest recognized human occupation in Texas.
The Paleo-Indian period across the entire North Texas region (ca. 9500–7000 B.C.) generally
includes those remains of human presence that can be dated to the very late Pleistocene and the
immediate post-Pleistocene periods (for recent reviews of this period in North Texas, see Johnson
1989; Peter et al. 1991:Appendix I; and Story 1990). As a matter of convenience, the period can
be subdivided into an early Paleo-Indian period (ca. 9500–8500 B.C.) and a late Paleo-Indian
period (8500–7000 B.C.).

20

In Northeast Texas, numerous diagnostic projectile points, such as Clovis, Plainview, Dalton,
Scottsbluff, and San Patrice, have been recovered as isolated surface finds or excavated in later
contexts (Carley n.d.; Perttula 1988a:17), but unfortunately, few Paleo-Indian sites in good
stratigraphic context have been found (Perttula 1988a:17; Preston 1972, 1974), and fewer have
received any sort of systematic excavation. Both Perttula and Story note the possible presence of
horizontally stratified early Paleo-Indian deposits at the Forrest Murphey site (41MR62) at Lake
O‘ the Pines, but the site was reportedly destroyed by dam construction before being excavated
(Perttula 1988a:17; Perttula et al. 1986:47; Story 1990:184–185). The situation of the Murphey
site and the discovery of the deeply buried Clovis-aged Aubrey site (41DN479) along the Elm
Fork of the Trinity River in Northcentral Texas (Ferring 1989, 1990, 2001) suggest that wellpreserved Paleo-Indian sites in Northeast Texas will only be found by examining deeply stratified
Pleistocene terraces or by penetrating more recent Holocene Alluvium in modern floodplains.
Despite the lack of good data relating to the early Paleo-Indian period in Northeast Texas, some
attempts have been made to generalize regarding settlement mobility and intensity of site
occupation, drawing on the limited data that are known and on assumptions based on
comparisons with other areas. For instance, a number of researchers have seen evidence for a
high degree of group mobility in the broad distribution of Paleo-Indian artifacts over the
landscape and in the variety of presumably nonlocal lithic raw materials from which they were
made (Meltzer and Smith 1986; Shafer 1977; Story 1990:177). Likewise, the well-documented
exploitation of large megafauna by Paleo-Indians in the western United States, coupled with the
known presence of similar species in Northeast Texas between 11,000 and 9,000 years ago (see
Hemmings 1983; Slaughter and Hoover 1963), has resulted in the popular conclusion that ―big
game hunting‖ was part of the Paleo-Indian subsistence strategy in Northeast Texas. Although a
possible association between a Clovis point and mastodon remains at the Murphey site (Story
1990:185) does support this likelihood, increasing evidence from sites to the west (such as the
Aubrey site) suggests that Paleo-Indian groups were less dependent upon ―big game hunting‖
than has been assumed in the past.
The late Paleo-Indian period in Northeast Texas appears to be distinguished by the divergence of
the earlier, widespread fluted point tradition into several distinct subtraditions. The first of these
includes Scottsbluff, Plainview, and similar lanceolate points that appear to be part of a more
western or ―plains-derived‖ subtradition in terms of origin and style; the second includes Dalton
and Dalton-related projectile points that have a wide distribution throughout the wooded
southeastern and midwestern United States. Some researchers have suggested that this Dalton
horizon represents an adaptation to the changing environment at the end of the Pleistocene
(Goodyear 1982:389–391), a view that has found some support in the addition of the Dalton adze,
a presumed ―heavy, woodworking tool,‖ to what otherwise is viewed as a Paleo-Indian tool kit
(Kelley et al. 1988:21). San Patrice, an important complex that may be related to Dalton, is found
in eastern Texas, southeastern Oklahoma, northern Louisiana, and southern Arkansas and is
characterized by San Patrice and Keithville points, and Albany scrapers (Ensor 1987; Schambach
1979; Webb et al. 1971).

Archaic Period
With the end of the Ice Age, the prehistoric residents of the region began to develop into localized
populations of efficient hunter-gatherers, exploiting localized resource bases. This period, and
the subsistence pattern that characterizes it, has become known as the Archaic. The Archaic
21

represents a long period of time that was characterized by only gradual and minor changes in
subsistence patterns, lithic technology, and projectile point styles and was apparently a period of
strong cultural stability. Archaic populations are usually portrayed as generalized hunters and
gatherers with more limited geographic ranges than the preceding Paleo-Indian peoples. At the
present time, there is no evidence for the development of local cultigens during the Archaic
period in Texas, although this is not the case for the Ozark Highlands and other parts of the
eastern United States.
The Archaic period in Northeast Texas is tentatively dated between 7000 and 200 B.C. As is true
for many areas, a threefold division of the Archaic period, consisting of Early, Middle, and Late
subperiods, has been applied in Northeast Texas. Although reliable dating for the Archaic period
in this area of Texas is virtually nonexistent, these divisions have been given tentative dates based
on better-dated sites in surrounding areas. Thus, the Early Archaic has been dated from 7000 to
4000 B.C., the Middle Archaic from 4000 to 2000 B.C., and the Late Archaic from 2000 to 200
B.C. (overviews that cover the Archaic in this portion of Texas include Fields and Tomka 1993;
Peter et al. 1991:Appendix I; and Story 1985, 1990). Archaic remains are usually found in
upland settings and are frequently mixed with later material (Campbell et al. 1983; Story 1981).
General trends that have been proposed as characterizing the Archaic period in Northeast Texas
include an increasingly complex settlement system, increasing population size and density,
increasing sedentism, and the development of distinct group territories (Perttula 1988a:17; Story
1985:52). Despite these changes, however, no evidence of any level of food production (even
incipient production) has been reported from any Northeast Texas Archaic site (Perttula
1988a:17; Story 1990:Table 56), in spite of the fact that definite steps toward food production
were being taken elsewhere in the eastern United States (Ford 1985:347–349; Watson 1988).
During the Early Archaic (ca. 7000–4000 B.C.), the occurrence of small and widely distributed
sites has been suggested to reflect high group mobility within large and poorly defined territories
and based on a generalized hunting and gathering economy (Meltzer and Smith 1986; Story
1985:35, 39). Projectile point forms that may be associated with the Early Archaic in Northeast
Texas include Kirk, Keithville, Palmer, Cossatot, Dawson, and Wells (Story 1990; Thurmond
1990).
In comparison to the Early Archaic, the Middle Archaic period (4000–2000 B.C.) appears to be
characterized by (1) an increased diversity of tool types; (2) greater interregional variability; (3)
the addition of ground, pecked, and polished stone tools; and (4) an increased use of plant foods
as indicated by the addition of mortars, pestles, and milling stones (Neuman 1984:77, 79). The
dependence upon abundant forest resources (e.g., oak mast production, deer, and small mammals)
that are evenly distributed over most of the region probably resulted in evenly distributed
population densities and favored the development of exclusive or ―fixed‖ territories (Plog and
Upham 1983:202; Story 1985:41). Although grinding stones apparently were introduced during
the Early Archaic period, it was not until the Middle Archaic that their use became widespread.
Grinding and polishing were used to produce grooved axes, atlatl weights, and ground stone
pendants during that period. Diagnostic dart points that may be associated with the Middle
Archaic include Big Sandy, Calf Creek, Johnson, Carrollton, Morrill, Evans, Lone Oak, Trinity,
and Wesley (Story 1990; Thurmond 1990).
Population density may have reached a peak during the Late Archaic period in Northeast Texas
(ca. 2000–200 B.C.) as evidenced by an apparent increase in the number of sites, a greater
distribution of sites over the landscape, and evidence of increasing degrees of sedentism. At the
22

same time, group mobility may have become more limited and interregional contact may have
become much more common. If the greater spatial dispersal of sites is not a result of sampling
error due to unequal destruction or burial of earlier sites, it may reflect an economic system with
increasing reliance on all available floral and faunal resources, although the economic data to
support this view are generally absent. Throughout Northeast Texas, Late Archaic period sites
are relatively common in the uplands, and a number are known from the floodplain of the Red
River as well as Late Archaic components in sites to the south near Lake Sam Rayburn in the
Neches/Angelina river area (Kenmotsu and Perttula 1993), but no regional phases have yet been
identified (Schambach 1982a:3–6). Dart points that may be diagnostic of the Late Archaic
include Lange, Castroville, Ellis, Palmillas, Edgewood, Yarbrough, Ensor, and Kent (Story 1990;
Thurmond 1990).

Early Ceramic Period
The Early Ceramic period (200 B.C.–A.D. 800) in Northeast Texas is generally not well defined
and is largely identified by similarities in pottery and projectile points with sites of the Fourche
Maline tradition north of the Red River (recent overviews that provide good information for this
period include Perttula et al. 1993; Peter et al. 1991:Appendix I; and Story 1990). Diagnostic
artifacts consist of coarse plainware ceramics, tempered with either clay/grog or bone, and Gary
projectile points. The ceramics generally are grouped together as Williams Plain; but given the
wide range of temporal and spatial variability present, they should probably be viewed as one or
more undefined varieties of that type. Discounting ceramic differences at the varietal level, the
remains of the Early Ceramic period in Northeast Texas seem to be most closely related to
Schambach‘s (1982b:188, 1998) Fourche Maline tradition in Arkansas. Despite other similarities
to Fourche Maline, however, no Early Ceramic period burial mounds are known in the Texas
portion of the Great Bend. The few that are known in East Texas occur to the south in the Sabine
and Neches river basins around the Toledo Bend and Sam Rayburn reservoir areas (see Story
1990:Figure 41) at the Coral Snake (16SA48; on the eastern edge of Toledo Bend in Louisiana)
and Jonas Short (41SA101) mound sites (Jensen 1968; McClurkan et al. 1966, 1980). The lack of
such evidence in Northeast Texas leaves open the question of whether or not this area was
undergoing the same processes of cultural evolution presumably responsible for the development
of burial mound use elsewhere (see Perttula 1988a:18; Story 1990).
Perttula (1988a:18) has noted what appears to be a major presence of Early Ceramic period
occupation within the Sulphur River basin, although this may be more the result of intensity of
research or factors of site preservation rather than the actual presence of a large Early Ceramic
population. In regard to modeling settlement patterns during this period, Perttula (1988a:18)
suggests that ―Early Ceramic or Fourche Maline settlements are . . . represented by villages and
hamlets in the floodplains or terraces of larger streams, and by smaller components in the
uplands.‖ Many of the sites on which Perttula bases this model, including Snipes at Wright
Patman Lake (Jelks 1961) and Tick, Thomas, Hurricane Hill, and Lawson at Cooper Lake
(Doehner and Larson 1978; Martin 1996a, 1996b; Perttula 1990), contain middens that are taken
as indicating a more sedentary settlement pattern than that of the preceding Archaic period. Two
apparent pits (Features 1 and 2) radiocarbon dated to the Early Ceramic period (1,460 ± 60 B.P.
and 2,090 ± 30 B.P., respectively) at 41HP137 at Cooper Lake yielded hickory nut, acorn, wild
tubers (possibly the prairie turnip, Pediomelum or Psoralea sp.), and what may be fragments of
cultivated squash or gourd (Fields et al. 1994:12; McGregor 1996:357).

23

Elsewhere in Northeast Texas, sandy paste ceramics (cf., Bear Creek Plain and Goose Creek
Plain) appear to be common on Early Ceramic period sites south of the Sabine River (Story
1981:146) along the upper reaches of the Angelina and Neches rivers, as well as farther south in
the Sam Rayburn and Toledo Bend reservoir areas. This pottery is technologically different from
the ceramics in the northern area of Northeast Texas. Many of the sites south of the Sabine (e.g.,
Deshazo [41NA27] and George C. Davis [41CE19], as well as Wolfshead [41SA117], Runnells 1
and 2 [41SA87 and SA86], and Dubose [41SA100] in the Sam Rayburn Reservoir area) exhibit
ceramics attributed to the Mossy Grove tradition (Kenmotsu and Perttula 1993:262, 233; Story
1990:277]). The indigenous Mossy Grove hunter-gatherer population was likely pushed
southward by Caddo groups at the end of the period (ca. A.D. 800/900) (Story 1990:256). Lithics
from these more southerly sites are predominantly the Kent and Gary dart point styles, along with
―rather nondescript bifaces . . . [that may represent] projectile point manufacture failures‖ and
some unifaces (Story 1990:256). Toward the middle Early Ceramic period (ca. A.D. 500–600),
the bow and arrow technology, along with the Friley arrow point, likely was introduced into the
more southeastern Texas region (Story 1990:256).

Caddo Period in the Great Bend-Neches/Angelina Regions
The Formative (A.D. 800–1000) and Early Caddo (A.D. 1000–1200) periods in the Great BendNeches/Angelina regions are not well defined. Ceramics for both of these periods include
Hickory Fine Engraved, Carmel Engraved, Crockett Curvilinear Incised, and Pennington
Punctated-Incised (Thurmond 1990:Table 8). Thurmond has further distinguished between
equivalents of these two periods on the basis of the presence of Davis Incised, Holly Fine
Engraved, Kiam Incised, Spiro Engraved, and Weches Fingernail Impressed in the earlier (along
with some late varieties of Coles Creek types), and of Canton Incised, Haley Engraved, Maxey
Noded Redware, Sanders Engraved, and Sanders Plain in the later, although some researchers are
more inclined to put the latter four types in the Middle Caddoan period.
The Formative and Early Caddoan periods are characterized by what may best be termed the Alto
complex or Alto sphere, a widespread manifestation related to the Alto phase, originally defined
at the George C. Davis site in Cherokee County, Texas, south of the Great Bend area (Newell and
Krieger 1949). The Alto complex shows strong influence from Coles Creek to the east and
appears to partially overlap it in time. It also, however, shows a number of new characteristics
(Neuman 1970), including new projectile point types (i.e., Hayes and Homan arrow points), new
ceramic vessel forms (i.e., the carinated bowl and the bottle), and a new mode of vessel
decoration (i.e., fine engraving with red pigment filler).
Perttula (1988a:18) has suggested that these and other cultural innovations, including the
introduction of the bow and arrow ca. A.D. 800 and increased food production based on maize, led
to increases in population and sociopolitical complexity during these periods. The settlement
system became increasingly complex, apparently involving sedentary villages and farmsteads,
special function sites (what Binford [1980] has called logistical camps), and mound centers that
were presumably ritual or ceremonial in function (see Perttula 1988a:18–19). A number of such
mound centers dating to the Formative and Early Caddo periods occur in Northeast Texas and
include the T. M. Coles or Mustang Creek Mound (Jackson 1931) on a tributary of the Sulphur
River to the west of Wright Patman Lake, and several mounds at the lake itself (Stephenson
1950). Many more mound sites occur along the Red River and its immediate tributaries to the
north (Banks 1983; Miller 1986; Taylor 1949). To the south, the Bivins-Flanagan mound (also
24

known as Hudnall-Pirtle; 41RK4) on the middle Sabine River is a major multiple mound site with
six to eight mounds, a plaza, and a habitation area (Kenmotsu and Perttula 1993:129). The
George C. Davis site in Cherokee County on the Neches River may be the best representative of
the mound center-village site type, another characteristic of the Alto focus. The Early Caddoan
component of this site likely indicates an emigrant group from the middle Sabine River area
(possibly Bivins-Flanagan?) arriving prior to ca. A.D. 900 (Story 1990:325).
The Middle Caddo period (A.D. 1200–1400) in the Great Bend region includes what is known as
the Haley phase, the definition of which is based generally on mortuary data, largely from C. B.
Moore‘s excavations at the Haley site (3MI1) in Arkansas (Moore 1912). The Haley phase
appears to represent a development out of the earlier Alto complex, and among the continuities
from the earlier period is the use of shaft grave burials for some members of the society who were
accompanied into the afterlife by relatively rich grave offerings (Kelley et al. 1988:26). This
phase was centered in the Great Bend area in Arkansas, but northwestern Louisiana and
northeastern Texas also fall within its peripheral influence. A Haley phase component has been
recognized at the Hatchel site in Bowie County (Davis 1970:44), and other Haley components are
known in the area, including the Cabe Mounds site (Perttula et al. 1995) and the Mitchell site
(Cliff et al. 1997). Coker Mound (41CS1) also apparently has a Haley phase component, based
on radiocarbon dates and a reported Haley Engraved bottle in a mortuary feature (Perttula,
Turner, and Nelson 1997:17–20). Despite its presence in Texas, however, the Haley phase was
apparently most fully elaborated in the Arkansas portion of the Great Bend.
Most of the well-known Haley phase components, especially in Arkansas, relate to mound
centers, although it is unclear whether all of them were associated with villages (Wyckoff 1974).
One well-investigated nonmound habitation site is known from Arkansas, apparently a small
farmstead located on ―a natural levee‖ or ―high ground overlooking riverine bottom lands‖
(Wyckoff 1974:106, 113). The site contained two circular house structures and a small trash
dump and has suggested to some that the Haley phase settlement pattern involved small,
dispersed farmsteads surrounding vacant ceremonial centers (Hoffman 1970). Wyckoff
(1974:107) also notes, however, the existence of mound centers with ―potentially large villages,‖
as well as nonmound cemeteries located close to mound centers. Finally, he states that:
[t]here is certainly an overall tendency for the Haley components, mound centers and non-mound
habitation areas alike, to associate with a riverine valley setting. Most of the mound centers did occur
in the valleys of major streams, but [some mound centers] were on the floodplains of streams
tributary to the major rivers [Wyckoff 1974:112].

In regard to sociopolitical organization within Haley phase society, the complexity of the
mortuary ceremonialism and apparent status ranking evident in the burials recovered from Haley
phase sites, as well as the presumed organizational control necessary to construct the mound
centers, strongly suggest that there was ―a political and religious hierarchy that operated
throughout the Haley Focus [sic] society‖ (Wyckoff 1974:110).
In the Sabine River area, the Oak Hill Village site (41RK214) in Rusk County (Rogers and
Perttula 2004), Washington Square (41NA49) in Nacogdoches County, Tyson (41SY92) in the
Attoyac River basin in Shelby County are among the major mound centers dating to the Early–
Middle Caddo period (Perttula 2000:24–25). Data from these sites suggest a horticultural
lifestyle based on maize and squash, supplemented by wild seeds, nuts, and tubers, along with
fishing and continued hunting of deer and other game. Mounds were used both as temple and
25

burial sites, and the larger centers were important civic-ceremonial areas. Many of the large
mound centers contained structures and plazas along with storage/granary structures and pits.
The distribution of the mound centers suggests an integrated society of rather complex sociopolitical extent. The Washington Square mound, securely dated to A.D. 1268 and 1302, was built
at the crossroads between aboriginal trails (later known as the east–west Caddo Trace and the
north–south Camino Real) and may have been primarily a ceremonial center since little indication
of permanent habitation has been documented. Radiocarbon dates also suggest the possibility
that Washington Square was the successor to George C. Davis, which was essentially abandoned
by the early fourteenth century (Perttula 2000:24–29).
Material culture reflects corner-notched and rectangular-stemmed arrow points, celts, Gahagan
bifaces, scrapers, and borer/perforators, as well as ceramic pipes, earspools, and figurines. The
decorated carinated, boat-shaped, cylindrical, and globular bowls and jars; bottles; plates and
platters; and ollas ceramics, however, are the most distinctive cultural items. These ceramics
were used for various food-related tasks—cooking, serving, storage—as well as for use as
personal items and ceremonial beakers in mortuary occasions (Perttula 2000:26–27).
The Late Caddo period (A.D. 1400–1680) includes the final part of the prehistoric period and the
initial years of European contact (for an overview of this period and the subsequent Historic
Caddo period, see Perttula 1992). The survivors of the de Soto entrada apparently entered
Northeast Texas about midway through the Late Caddo period, the latter part of which appears to
have overlapped with the initial movements of seventeenth-century European explorers and
traders into northeastern Texas.
In the lower Sulphur River basin, two archeological complexes have been defined for the Late
Caddo period, the Titus phase and the Texarkana phase (Schambach 1982a; Thurmond 1985).
The Titus phase appears to be centered between the Sabine and Sulphur rivers in the Northeast
Texas piney woods and post-oak savanna. The Texarkana phase is located in the upper portion of
the Great Bend of the Red River and extends southward to include a portion of the lower Sulphur
River drainage (Wyckoff 1974:Figure 4). The definition of the Texarkana phase is based largely
on WPA excavations conducted at the Hatchel mound and at the Mitchell and Moores cemeteries
situated on the Red River northwest of Texarkana (Davis 1970:50–51). Texarkana phase sites,
such as Sherwin on the lower Sulphur River, probably existed as satellites to these larger,
permanent settlements on the Red River (Perttula 1988a).
Both the Titus and Texarkana phases appear to be characterized by small hamlets or farmsteads
that were probably occupied by small family groups of shifting agriculturalists. These farmsteads
were apparently characterized by a limited number of structures and a small family cemetery (see
Brewington and Dockall 1991; Brewington et al. 1995; Jelks 1961; Perttula 1988a; and Wormser
1988). They presumably were associated with larger, more permanent suprahousehold sites,
including both mound centers and nonmound cemeteries, which served to integrate the scattered
households into a unified social group.
In the Neches/Angelina river basin, the Frankston (A.D. 1400–1650) and Angelina (post-A.D.
1450/1500) phases are dominant. In this area, there is a noticeable lack of Late Caddo large
mound burials after ca. A.D. 1400. Additionally, the lack of elaborate burials, especially as
indicated at the George C. Davis site, may indicate a decline in organizational hierarchy (Story
1990:340). The Frankston phase A. C. Saunders site (41AN19) near the Neches River in
Anderson County is the only known mound site. Frankston phase habitation sites consist
26

primarily of farmsteads, hamlets, and small villages (Perttula 2000:31) and many cluster along
the upper Neches River (Anderson 1972:163). Burials tend to be in small (possibly) family or
village cemeteries of less than 10 individuals, with many interments containing ceramic grave
goods. To the south near Sam Rayburn Reservoir, the Angelina focus sites yielded ceramic
elbow pipes, and brushed and punctated ceramics. Perdiz arrow points, conch shell beads, bone
tools, and worked bone artifacts have been recovered from burials at the Walter Bell site
(41SB50) in Sabine County (Jelks 1965:56–60). In the Toledo Bend Reservoir area in Sabine and
Shelby counties, Late Caddo residential settlements exhibit extensive middens and small
cemeteries that were contemporaneous with Angelina phase sites to the west (Perttula 2000:32).

Caddo Period in the Big Cypress Creek Basin
The time span between the Archaic and the Caddo period (i.e., the Early Ceramic from ca. 200
B.C.–A.D. 800) in the Cypress Creek basin is poorly represented, but Caddo habitation of the
period subsequent to ca. A.D. 800 is well documented. The Caddo period in the Cypress Creek
basin has been subdivided into early, middle, and late subperiods: the Early Caddo from ca. A.D.
800 to 1300, the Middle Caddo from ca. A.D. 1300 to 1400, and the Late Caddo from A.D. 1400 to
1680 (see Thurmond 1990:39–40). Thurmond‘s (1990) Early Caddo period includes components
related to the Alto and Sanders foci and is subdivided into two parts. Ceramic types characteristic
