long. After completion of the BHT unit excavation, the unit profile was inspected for cultural
deposits and the soil matrix was described and recorded photographically. The placement of
BHT units was made judgmentally by the investigators to provide the most extensive coverage of
the recommended survey area.
All excavation units were backfilled after documentation. All materials generated by these
investigations (artifacts, documents, photographs, etc.) will be permanently housed at TARL at
the conclusion of this contract.

ELIGIBILITY CRITERIA

National Register of Historic Places
The assessment of significance of cultural resources properties is based on federal guidelines.
The criteria to evaluate properties for inclusion in the NRHP are codified under the authority of
the National Historic Preservation Act of 1966. Based on regulations set forth by the Advisory
Council on Historic Preservation (ACHP), any resource that is included in or eligible for
inclusion in the National Register is a ―historic property‖ (36 CFR 800.16[1][1]) According to
federal regulations, ―The term ‗eligible for inclusion in the National Register‘ includes both
properties formally determined as such by the Secretary of the Interior and all other properties
that meet National Register listing criteria‖ (36 CFR 800.16[l][2]). The ACHP has set forth the
following regulations to define significance and to determine eligibility of cultural resources:
The quality of significance in American history, architecture, archeology, engineering, and culture is
present in districts, sites, buildings, structures, and objects that possess integrity of location, design,
setting, material, workmanship, feeling, and association and
(a) that are associated with events that have made a significant contribution to the broad patterns of
our history; or
(b) that are associated with the lives of persons significant in our past; or
49

(c) that embody the distinctive characteristics of a type, period, or method of construction, or that
represent the work of a master, or that possess high artistic values, or that represent a significant
and distinguishable entity whose components may lack individual distinction; or
(d) that have yielded or may be likely to yield, information important in prehistory or history [39 CFR
Part 60].

Texas State Archeological Landmark
Under the Texas Antiquities Code at the state level, archeological sites may be considered
significant and be recognized or designated as an SAL, provided that at least one of the following
conditions is met:
(1) The archeological site is situated on lands owned or controlled by the State of Texas or one of its
political subdivisions; or
(2) The archeological site is situated on private lands that have been specifically designated as an
SAL . . . and fits at least one of the following criteria:
(A) Preservation of materials must be sufficient to allow application of standard archeological
techniques to advantage;
(B) The majority of artifacts are in place so that a significant portion of the site‘s original
characteristics can be defined through investigation;
(C) The site has the potential to contribute to cumulative culture history by the addition of new
information;
(D) The site offers evidence of unique or rare attributes; and/or
(E) The site offers a unique and rare opportunity to test techniques, theory, or methods or
preservation, thereby contributing to scientific knowledge [Texas Natural Resources Code
1977; Title 9, Chapter 191, Texas Antiquities Committee, Section 191.094 and Chapter
41.7, Antiquities Code of Texas].

SITE DEFINITION
During this contract, a site was defined on the basis of content and extent. When a shovel test
yielded cultural material, additional shovel tests were excavated in a cruciform (when possible) or
linear pattern within the APE, generally at approximately 10-m (33-ft) intervals, from the initial
test until two sterile shovel tests were encountered in each direction. For surficial materials, a site
was defined as five or more cultural items of at least two different artifact materials or classes
within a 20-m (66-ft) square. The site limit was then delimited within the extent of positive
shovel tests and/or surface artifact scatter. Cultural remains meeting these criteria were
designated as a site, recorded on a Texas Archeological Site Data Form, and submitted to TARL.
Conversely, the discovery of one or two cultural items (either surface or subsurface) not meeting
these criteria was considered an isolated occurrence of human activity and was simply
documented by location and content as a ―locality‖; likewise, historic-era material representing an
obvious single-event trash dump was not considered a site, with only location and content
documented.

IMPACTS AND SITE POTENTIAL
One of the principal concerns of both impact assessments and surveys is the identification of
archeological historic properties (36 CFR 800.16.[1]) and SALs (13 TAC 26.12). Disturbances,
whether natural or man-made (Table 3), diminish the possibility of finding such properties. In the
impact evaluation phase, assessing disturbances can assist in identifying areas where further
survey is not necessary. Conversely, identifying areas possessing a high potential for containing
archeological historic properties or SALs is facilitated by an awareness of the effects of
disturbance. Whether classified as man-made fill, man-made cut, or naturally occurring, certain
disturbances occur in existing ROWs with such frequency that it is possible to form a few
generalized observations regarding their extent and effect on the landscape.
Cut-and-fill episodes occur when a road or bridge approach is elevated above the floodplain. Fill
for the road berm is often obtained from the margins within the ROW and deposited in the center
of the ROW. The result is a raised road berm bordered by depressed areas, which function to
facilitate drainage runoff. If the slope at the junction of the terrace above the floodplain with the
road or bridge is steep or sudden, a road cut is sometimes used to lessen the incline without
raising the road higher. For roads situated parallel with the slope, a road cut on the high side of

the slope is sometimes necessary for a level road. Fill from the road cut may be deposited on the
low side, or used elsewhere. At the immediate junction of the road or bridge with the terrace, a
variety of methods can be employed to hold the shoulders in place against erosion. These can
include concrete or stone aprons or less formally designed sheet metal or wooden aprons.
Road berms are generally 1–3 m (3–10 ft) in height on narrow rural roads, but can be up to 5–6 m
(16–20 ft) in height on major highways or over wide floodplains. The extent of the raised berm
shoulders can be quite wide, ranging from 10 m (33 ft), which are the limits of the ROW on
narrow, one-lane, rural roads, to 20–23 m (66–75 ft) on larger roads. Borrow areas or drainage
ditches are approximately 0.5–1.0 m (2–3 ft) deep on smaller roads, but can extend up to 1.5 m (5
ft). Both trenching and shovel testing reveal that disturbances from earth-working equipment can
extend 0.5 m (2 ft) and deeper.
Underground utilities are typically placed outside the limits of road berms, near the margins of
the ROW, and can be stacked vertically or spaced horizontally. Telephone, fiber optic, and
electrical lines can be laid shallowly in narrow trenches of approximately 1 m (3 ft) in width.
Fiber optic cables are laid farthest from the road, usually at the outer edge of the ROW and
outside of older phone and electric cables. In addition, gas and water lines exposed at stream
crossings have been observed at depths of more than 4 m (13 ft). Sewer and fire hydrant, as well
as gas and water line, placement is a consideration in residential and industrial areas, though these
are not typically marked. Aboveground utility transmission lines are typically placed toward the
margins of the ROW. Within any given ROW, it is possible that 1–3 m (3–10 ft) of horizontal
utility installation disturbances exist.
Because of their association with floodplains, many of these project areas would naturally
undergo depositional processes due to periodic alluviation. Man-made disturbances, however,
exacerbate the natural effects of erosion by altering drainage patterns. Bridge aprons, road berms,
and drainage ditches channel rain runoff through the ROW, causing intense erosion of surface
soils, cutbanks, and terrace edges adjacent to these features. The barbed-wire fence, a common
feature in rural areas, traps debris in its mesh and thus hinders surface water from draining into
contiguous areas. Another trap along creeks is the wire or slat-and-wire ―fence‖ strung across a
61

creek to trap large debris at bridges and culverts. The backup of large debris tends to cause
extensive erosion at the edges of bridges or culverts compromising the structure as well as the
road crossing it. Utility trenches are typically backfilled with the original matrix, and these
loosened soils erode quickly in areas of heightened runoff.
Other disturbances that have been observed in existing ROWs as well as the adjacent areas and
therefore have the potential to be located in new ROWs are usually associated with residential,
commercial, or rural development. In residential areas, improvements can include landscaping,
improved drainages, culvert driveways, curb cuts, and construction. Commercial improvements
that have been encountered in project areas have been railroad beds, dams and their associated
reservoirs, berms, clear cutting, and trash dumping. In rural areas, evidence of plowing, logging,
ranching, and stock pond construction is apparent. Also note that the first stage of
highway/bridge construction consists of vegetation removal by bulldozer in order to remove both
trees and roots.

64

SITE 41BW166 (WA 578 20 SA001 / CSJ 0085-04-034)

Previous Investigations
Site 41BW166 was previously recorded in 1979 as a prehistoric lithic scatter plotted
approximately 120 m east of the proposed US 259 project APE on the Pleistocene terrace
escarpment above Mill Creek (to the north) and Tanyard Creek (to the southwest). The accuracy
of the plotted location was uncertain because of inconsistencies in the site description on the site
form (McCormick 1979a), but the plotted location seemed plausible considering the overall
information provided on the site form. This site was described as a scatter of lithic debris and
fire-cracked rock (FCR) bisected by a dirt road (possibly CR 3316). No diagnostic artifacts were
noted, but an Archaic temporal affiliation was suggested on the site form, possibly due to the lack
of ceramics at the site. Geographic information system (GIS) data provided by TARL indicated
that 41BW166 extends into the APE. No other information about the site was available, and its
eligibility for inclusion in the NRHP or for designation as an SAL was unknown.

Current Investigations
Site 41BW166 was one of the sites investigated in conjunction with the proposed project to widen
an 11.9-mi segment of US 259 from just south of the Oklahoma/Texas state line to just north of
US 82 in De Kalb, Bowie County, Texas. The site is plotted on the De Kalb NW (33094-F6)
USGS 7.5' quadrangle. The APE is defined as the existing 61-m-wide maximum ROW owned by
the state of Texas. Fieldwork for the 2010 cultural resources survey was undertaken in April and
June 2010.
Site 41BW166 is situated on the Pleistocene terrace escarpment above and south of Mill Creek
(near Mill Creek‘s confluence with Tanyard Creek to the west) in an area dissected by numerous
unnamed tributaries of Mill and Tanyard creeks. This area is characterized by broad, gentle rises.
The site area adjacent to the APE is predominantly open pasture or agricultural fields; a small
wooded area to the north extends into the northern half of the site. Vegetation within the APE
consists primarily of tall grasses. The underlying geology of the site, situated on the northern
edge the Pleistocene terrace escarpment, is mapped as Pleistocene Fluviatile Terrace deposits
(BEG 1975). Soils in the vicinity of the site are mapped as Darden loamy fine sand, 1 to 8
percent slopes (Soil Survey Staff 2010). A representative shovel test revealed 20 cm of very pale
brown (10YR 7/4) fine sandy loam over 80 cm of reddish yellow (7.5YR 6/6) fine sandy loam.
Within the APE, contextual integrity has been compromised by road construction and
maintenance activities and possibly by agricultural use of the adjacent areas.
Previously recorded site 41BW166 was identified during the current survey when positive shovel
tests were encountered along the northern Pleistocene terrace escarpment landform within the
APE (Figure 6). Forty-one shovel tests were excavated on two linear transects, one along each
side of the highway, to determine the site boundary. Recovered cultural materials extend the
previously recorded site boundary at least 60 m to the west, across the present project APE. CR
3316 intersects the ROW about midway along the eastern side of the site, with a wooded area to
the north within the ROW.

65

The recovery of a total of 60 artifacts in 21 positive shovel tests in the eastern and western ROWs
extends the previously recorded site boundary to the west by at least 60 m, across the APE,
covering an area within the APE of approximately 215 m NW–SE by 60 m NE–SW. The
artifacts collected from site 41BW166 consist of 49 prehistoric lithics, a sample of four charcoal
(nutshell) specimens, and seven historic artifacts. The prehistoric lithics assemblage is composed
primarily of 43 pieces of debitage (biface thinning flakes [n=24], decortication flakes [n=11], and
shatter [n=8]) collected from 0–110 cmbs). Quartzite (n=19) and undifferentiated chert (n=17)
comprise the majority of the debitage raw material, with lesser amounts of novaculite (n=4) and
chalcedony, translucent chert, and quartz (n=1 each). The only tested cobble from the project was
recovered from Transect 1/Shovel Test 75, 60–80 cmbs; the specimen is of quartzite. Four pieces
of FCR were found at 0–20 and 60–80 cmbs). A contracting-stemmed quartzite biface as
collected from Transect 1/Shovel Test 50 (40–60 cmbs), possibly representing a dart point, but
more likely a knife based on the asymmetrical resharpening of the blade, indicating a site
occupation during the Late Archaic period. Also from this same shovel test, the sample of four
pieces of burned nutshell (80–100 cmbs) was collected. The historic artifacts, recovered from
three shovel tests (20–60 cmbs), consist of three pieces of clear bottle glass (post-1880), one
fragment of ash tint vessel glass, and a 4-hole button of compressed paper. The button as cheaply
produced, probably for pants, likely in the mid to late nineteenth century (Winchester Sutler, Inc.,
2010). No intact cultural features were encountered during shovel testing.

Recommendations
Site 41BW166 is situated on a terrace above Mill Creek in an area of broad, gentle rises. As
originally recorded, the site lies primarily to the east of the current APE. However, positive
shovel tests with possible Archaic material, based on the recovered stemmed dart projectile
point/knife, have extended the site boundary westward across the current APE. Although no
intact cultural features were found during shovel testing, the preservation and recovery of burned
nutshell fragments and FCR indicate that such features may be present at this site. Based on the
findings that the site may retain intact cultural features and cultural materials recovered from
survey, the portion of site 41BW166 within the APE may retain research potential relevant to
Archaic settlement and subsistence patterns in the uplands of the Red River basin. Therefore,
additional intensive archeological investigations are recommended in the portion of 41BW166
within the APE to determine the presence, extent, and integrity of intact cultural deposits.

SITE 41BW761 (WA 578 11 SA001 AND WA 578 17 SA001 / CSJ 0919-19-052)

Current Investigations
Site 41BW761 is in the northwest quadrant of Anderson Creek and CR 4320 in Bowie County,
plotted on the 1975 Hodgson 7.5' series USGS quadrangle. The existing ROW is owned by the
state of Texas, but proposed construction easements will extend into privately owned property, to
which right-of-entry access was granted. The APE for the proposed TxDOT project has a
maximum width of approximately 61 m. The site was first identified during fieldwork on August
25, 2009, and additional fieldwork was conducted on October 7 and November 4–5, 2009.

67

The site is situated in the undeveloped, periodically inundated floodplain of Anderson Creek on a
small remnant terrace overlooking the creek to the west and its unnamed tributary to the north of
the knoll. The vegetation in the site vicinity consists of some grasses and forbs, but the majority
is secondary hardwood forest composed primarily of oak, elm, and hickory trees, with a limited
understory vegetation of shrubs and vines. The geology in the area consists of Holocene
Alluvium, flanked to the north and south by undifferentiated deposits of the Upper Cretaceous
Navarro Group (BEG 1979). Soil survey maps indicate that the site area is mapped on Thenas
fine sandy loam, frequently flooded. Onsite Shovel Test 4 revealed 20 cm of sandy loam, over
20 cm of very fine sandy loam, over 10 cm of clay.
During the initial field investigations, a positive shovel test in the northwest quadrant (in the
privately owned proposed construction easement) prompted additional subsurface investigation of
the quadrant to determine if an archeological site was present and, if so, to delineate its boundary
(Figure 7). The initial positive shovel test (Shovel Test 4) yielded one chert flake between 10 and
40 cmbs, instigating a subsequent cruciform pattern of radial shovel tests. The two initial shovel
tests (Shovel Tests 5 and 6) to the northwest of Shovel Test 4 each contained cultural materials.
Negative shovel tests to the northwest and the southeast and CR 4320 to the south, an eroding
channel east of the terrace, and the creek to the west delineated the site boundary, with a site size
of approximately 15-x-10 m within the APE. The three positive shovel tests on the eroding
terrace yielded a piece of charcoal and a chert flake from Shovel Test 4 at 40 cmbs, a chert flake
from 10–40 cmbs in Shovel Test 5, and six chert flakes (four from 20–40 cmbs, and one each at
40–60 cmbs and at 60–80 cmbs) from Shovel Test 6. Because all archeological materials
discovered on this particular project were on privately owned property, all archeological materials
were photographed and reburied in their original shovel tests. To aid relocation of the artifacts,
the materials were placed in plastic zip-lock bags or film canisters prior to reburial. The locations
of the shovel tests were then recorded with a GPS device, and a pace-and-compass map of the site
was drawn.
The overall integrity of the deposits in the APE was considered to be low because the area was
within a dynamic floodplain environment with unstable surfaces. The only area that appeared to
maintain a minimal degree of integrity was the eroding terrace (approximately 15–20 m in
diameter) within the northwest quadrant where site 41BW761 was encountered. Because of the
erosion, the low density of artifacts, the lack of diagnostic materials, and the general floodplain
context, the site was considered not eligible for NRHP inclusion.
Nonetheless, TxDOT subsequently considered additional work was needed at the site to
determine if the low artifact densities and lack of diagnostic artifacts resulting from the original
survey stemmed from the site‘s limited research potential or from the small sample size
recovered. In early November 2009, Geo-Marine archaeologists revisited the site. Five
additional shovel tests were excavated around Shovel Tests 5 and 6, and two 1-x-1-m test units
were excavated: one test unit adjacent to Shovel Test 6 at the top of the terrace, and the other on
the mid-slope of the terrace adjacent to Shovel Test 5.
Two of the additional shovel tests (Shovel Tests 12 and 15) and both test units yielded additional
cultural materials. Shovel Test 12 yielded two chert flakes (one cortical, one interior) from 0–20
cmbs, and Shovel Test 15 yielded a red quartzite cobble that was either a tested quartzite core or a
naturally fractured stream cobble. The two 1-x-1-m test units yielded a total of 124 lithic
artifacts, 20 charcoal fragments, and three FCR. Test Unit 1 located on top of the terrace yielded

68

91 lithic artifacts, 13 charcoal fragments, and three FCR; Test Unit 2 located near the mid-slope
of the terrace yielded 33 lithic artifacts and seven charcoal fragments. The lithic materials
included numerous raw material types, most likely collected from local stream gravels, including
fine and coarse-grained white and pink quartzites, a wide range of black, tan/brown, and gray
cherts, and two translucent chert flakes resembling novaculite or chalcedony. Sixty-eight percent
of the total flakes recovered from both test units combined were classified as interior flakes (73
percent from Test Unit 1 and 63 percent from Test Unit 2) suggesting that the site inhabitants
participated in later stages of tool manufacture and/or rejuvenation of existing tools. No features
or temporally diagnostic cultural materials were recovered; however, an incomplete,
nondiagnostic biface was discovered in Test Unit 1 imbricated among stream gravels at 80 cmbs.
It is possible that this biface was used as a knife because one of the edges was left 4–5
millimeters (mm) below the midline, a trait common to prehistoric cutting implements. Three
scrapers and one retouched flake were recovered from Test Unit 1, suggesting that floral and/or
faunal resources were also procured at the site. Finally, a chert preform was recovered from 30–
40 cmbs in Test Unit 2.
As indicated by the stream gravels recovered in almost every level within both test units
excavated at 41BW761, it is likely that the terrace has formed in association with overbank
flooding of Anderson Creek. This process appears to have occurred often enough through time to
prevent stable surfaces from being established, since no distinct bedding or repeatable structures
were observed within shovel tests or test units. Further compromising the integrity of the cultural
deposits at the site is evidence for significant erosion of the terrace. It appears that the ground
surface near Test Unit 2, excavated 30–40 cm downslope from Test Unit 1, has been removed
through natural erosional processes and that the ground surfaces of Test Unit 1 and Test Unit 2 at
some point in time existed at similar elevations. For example, the majority of the artifacts
recovered from Test Unit 1 (around 65 percent) were found between 40 and 80 cmbs, and the
majority of the artifacts recovered from Test Unit 2 (around 90 percent) were found between 10
and 50 cmbs. Additionally, this 40-cm-thickness range in Test Unit 1 and Test Unit 2 (40–80
cmbs and 10–50 cmbs, respectively), consisted of similar natural soil layers composed of 10YR
5/4 sands and 10YR 6/3 and 10YR 6/4 sandy loams. However, no distinct bedding was observed
within this 40-cm-thickness range and not all of the artifacts recovered were confined within this
range. As a result, it is likely that artifacts have been mixed and translocated throughout the soil
matrix in association with natural processes of erosion and deposition. Further clarifying this
interpretation was the recovery of the nondiagnostic biface imbricated among stream gravels at
80 cmbs in Test Unit 1.
Additional disturbances were evidenced by a highly eroded ditch feeding into the southwest
quadrant and inferred by evidence that CR 4320 was bermed to approximately 1 m above the
natural ground surface during construction. The northwest quadrant has also been affected by
disturbance from construction of a road ditch lined with cobbles (likely to prevent erosion), as
well as buried and overhead utilities within the APE.

Recommendations
An intensive archaeological survey identified site 41BW761 on an eroded, remnant terrace
overlooking Anderson Creek and an unnamed tributary along CR 4320. The lack of distinctive
bedding and absence of buried soils and distribution of artifacts within a thick depth range of
mixed alluvial deposits limit the site‘s research potential for contributing to the cumulative
70

culture history in northeast Texas. Additionally, the integrity of the site has been compromised
through significant erosion that has progressed on the boundary of the terrace and removed a
substantial portion of the upper matrix from the majority of the terrace. Further limiting the site‘s
research potential is evidence that artifacts are being translocated and imbricated within stream
gravel deposits. Based on these findings, site 41BW761 does not retain the contextual integrity
or research potential to warrant consideration for further investigation or for in-place protection
or for inclusion in the NRHP or for designation as an SAL.

SITE 41BW766 (WA 578 20 SA001 / CSJ 0085-04-034)

Current Investigations
Site 41BW766 is one of the several sites identified and recorded during cultural resources survey
in conjunction with the proposed project to widen an 11.9-mi segment of US 259 from just south
of the Oklahoma/Texas state line to just north of US 82 in De Kalb, Bowie County, Texas. The
site is on the USGS De Kalb NW (33094-F6) 7.5' series quadrangle. The APE is defined as the
existing 61-m-wide maximum ROW, which is owned by the state of Texas. Fieldwork for the
cultural resources survey was undertaken in April and June 2010.
Site 41BW766 consists of a low-density lithic scatter situated on a small erosional remnant of the
Pleistocene terrace north of Mill Creek in the Red River valley. Pasture comprises the area
surrounding the APE; within the APE, the groundcover is primarily tall grasses and wildflowers.
The underlying geology of the site area is Undifferentiated Holocene Alluvium (BEG 1975).
Soils in the area are mapped as Ruston fine sandy loam, 3 to 8 percent slopes (Soil Survey Staff
2010). Transect 2/Shovel Test 30 revealed 20 cm of yellowish red (5YR 5/8) fine sandy loam
over 80 cm of red (2.5YR 5/8) fine sandy loam. Modern intrusive features at the site include a
fence and gate, an access road, buried utilities, and US 259.
Site 41BW766 was discovered when cultural material was encountered in two shovel tests on a
low rise along the western ROW (Figure 8). The positive shovel tests are approximately 40 m
apart, separated by a small road, on the western side of the ROW. Six additional shovel tests
were excavated in the area of the positive tests to define the site boundary.
Two shovel tests along the western ROW on the Pleistocene rise encountered six artifacts:
prehistoric lithic debris of siltstone (n=1) and quartzite (n=2) debitage (0–20 cmbs), FCR or
thermally altered rock (n=2; 40–60 cmbs); and one historic clear glass body sherd (0–20 cmbs).
Six delineation shovel tests in both the eastern and western ROWs were excavated in the effort to
define the site boundary, but no additional cultural materials were recovered. Cultural deposits
on the highest portion of the rise (approximately 335 ft above mean sea level [amsl]) are confined
to an area of 55 m NW–SE by 20 m NE–SW. No cultural features were encountered within the
APE.

71

Recommendations
Site 41BW766 is a very small scatter of lithic debris and two possible FCR (as well as one glass
fragment) on a small erosional remnant of the Pleistocene terrace north of Mill Creek. No
diagnostic material was recovered and no features were identified within the APE. The site likely
extends beyond the APE to the northeast and southwest into the adjacent pastures. However,
based on the current findings, the portion of the site within the APE has little research potential
due to the low density of artifacts recovered and the degree of modern disturbance in the area.
Therefore, no further investigation of the portion of the site within the APE is recommended.

SITE 41BW767 (WA 578 20 SA001 / CSJ 0085-04-034)

Current Investigations
Site 41BW767 is another of the several sites identified and recorded during cultural resources
survey in conjunction with the proposed project to widen an 11.9-mi segment of US 259 from just
south of the Oklahoma/Texas state line to just north of US 82 in De Kalb, Bowie County, Texas.
The site is plotted on the USGS De Kalb NW (33094-F6) 7.5' series quadrangle. The APE is
defined as the existing 61-m-wide maximum ROW, which is owned by the state of Texas.
Fieldwork for the cultural resources survey was undertaken in April and June 2010.
Site 41BW767 represents of a late nineteenth- to early twentieth-century domestic site with a
minimal prehistoric lithic scatter situated on a topographic rise on the otherwise flat-lying
Pleistocene terrace. The eastern portion of the site is adjacent to a mixed hardwood forest, and
the western portion of the site is adjacent to a pasture. The underlying geology of the site area
consists of Pleistocene Fluviatile Terrace deposits (BEG 1975). Soils in the area are mapped as
Smithdale fine sandy loam, 8 to 12 percent slopes (Soil Survey Staff 2010). A typical shovel test
consisted of brown (10YR 5/3) sandy loam in the upper 20 cm followed by pale brown (10YR
6/3) sand to a depth of approximately 40 cm over grayish brown (10YR 5/2) clay with reddish
brown (5YR 4/4) mottles. This site has been affected by the construction and maintenance of US
259, agricultural practices along the western margin of the APE, buried utilities along the eastern
margin of the APE, and residential development to the north.
Site 41BW767 was identified when cultural materials were recovered in a shovel test (Figure 9).
Additional shovel tests were excavated in the area of the positive tests to define the site
boundary; 13 tests yielded cultural materials. The positive shovel tests within the eastern and
western ROWs of the APE, southeast of CR 3314, encompass an area of approximately 162 m
NW-SE by 56 m NE-SW.
A total of 84 artifacts was collected: 82 historic artifacts and two prehistoric artifacts.
Architectural items (n=19) recovered include bricks (handmade [n=2] and machine-made [n=3]),
window glass (clear [n=1, post-1880; light green [n=6], and light tint [n=2]), wire nails (n=4,
post-1890), and a roofing nail. Domestic items (n=43) include a piece of natural clay slipped
interior/exterior stoneware (1875–1900), light blue tinted whiteware (n=2, 1880–1930), pure
white whiteware (n=1, post-1890), bottle glass (amber/brown [n=4], clear with ash tint [n=6,
post-1915], and clear [n=20, post-1880]), table glass (clear [n=2, post-1880] and cobalt blue
[n=1]), a light green body sherd, a milk glass jar neck, a possible lid to a coffee percolator, two
73

rolled tin can rims, and one crimped tin can rim (post-1902). Furnishings are represented by a
piece of electric lamp glass. Personal items are represented by a snuff jar fragment and a snap or
grommet for fastening material. Activities-related items consist of a piece of bar iron with two
protruding rivets, interpreted as a machine part. Unidentifiable pieces of iron (n=13), a piece of
rubber, a piece of tar, and a piece of slate are classified as Indeterminate. The majority of the
assemblage (n=80) came from the 0–20 cmbs level; a machine-made brick and a glass fragment
were the only materials from the 20–40 cmbs level. Prehistoric artifacts from the site consist of
jasper debitage (n=1, 0–20 cmbs) and a possible fragment of a ground stone artifact or FCR (n=1,
40–60 cmbs), both recovered in Transect 2/Shovel Test 42 excavated on the eastern margin of a
prairie mound.
Although no historic structure is presently located within the APE or in the immediate vicinity,
the 1951 USGS De Kalb NW topographic map shows a primary structure within the presently
existing US 259 roadway (see inset on Figure 9). A structure is also represented in this location
on a 1918 USDA Bowie County soils map. It is likely that the historic artifacts recovered at
41BW767, adjacent to the former location of this structure, are associated with activities related
to the building indicated on historic maps. This structure was likely demolished prior to or during
construction of US 259 in the early 1960s. No architectural features were encountered within the
APE, but site 41BW767 likely extends to the northeast and southwest beyond the APE.

Recommendations
Site 41BW767 represents a domestic site (with two prehistoric artifacts) situated on a Pleistocene
terrace south of Mill Creek. Based on earlier maps and dateable historic material, the site was
probably occupied in the late nineteenth to early twentieth centuries, but the structure on historic
maps was likely demolished prior to the early 1960s. Although the site yielded a relatively high
artifact density, the portion of the site within the APE has little research potential due to the
degree of disturbance in the area and the relatively undistinguished artifact assemblage. No
further investigation of the portion of site 41BW767 within the APE is recommended.

SITE 41BW768 (WA 578 20 SA001 / CSJ 0085-04-034)

Current Investigations
Site 41BW768 is the fourth of five sites identified and recorded during cultural resources survey
in conjunction with the proposed project to widen an 11.9-mi segment of US 259 from just south
of the Oklahoma/Texas state line to just north of US 82 in De Kalb, Bowie County, Texas. The
site is plotted on the USGS De Kalb NW (33094-F6) 7.5' series quadrangle. The APE is defined
as the existing 61-m-wide maximum ROW, which is owned by the state of Texas. Fieldwork for
the cultural resources survey was undertaken in April and July 2010.
Site 41BW768 consists of a late nineteenth- to early twentieth-century artifact scatter situated in a
level portion of the Pleistocene terrace south of a low-lying, periodically inundated area (Figure
10). Vegetation in the area is a mixed hardwood-pine forest adjacent to US 259. The underlying
geology of the site area consists of Pleistocene Fluviatile Terrace deposits (BEG 1975). The area
is mapped as Smithdale fine sandy loam, but shovel tests indicated that the northern portion of the
75

site likely consists of a Wrightsville series inclusion. Soils in the northern portion of the site
consist of a thin (5 cm) dark yellowish brown (10YR 4/6) silt loam over a very pale brown (10YR
8/2) silt loam to a depth of approximately 14 cm, underlain by brown (10YR 5/3) clay with red
(2.5YR 5/8) mottles. Conversely, soils on the southern portion of the site are a more
homogeneous light reddish brown (2.5YR 6/4) silt loam. The site has been affected by buried
utilities along its eastern margin, the construction and maintenance of US 259, and the managed
timberland to the south. An inundated area lies to the north, likely associated with an unnamed
tributary of Tanyard Creek.
Artifacts were recovered from 13 shovel tests in the eastern and western ROWs of the APE; the
eastern side of the APE at approximately 40 m north of the positive shovel tests was inundated
and disturbed and thus could not be shovel-tested. The site encompasses an area of
approximately 155 m NW-SE by 60 m E-W. Ten negative shovel tests defined the limits of the
site. A total of 95 historic artifacts was collected at site 41BW768. Architectural artifacts (n=54)
include brick (machine-made [n=1, post-1903] and handmade [n=49, pre-1903]), an iron
escutcheon plate, a cut nail (1840–1880), and two wire nails (post-1890). Domestic items (n=25)
are composed of a piece of Bristol-glazed interior/exterior stoneware (post-1900) and vessel glass
(amber/brown [n=8], aqua [n=1], clear [n=9], light green [n=1], light tint [n=2], and manganese
solarized [n=3]). Activities-related items include a fence staple, four Bakelite (1907–1940s)
fragments, and a modern plastic bottle cap (1942–present; 40–60 cmbs). An unidentifiable piece
of modern plastic (1942–present; 20–40 cmbs), three pieces of wire, two pieces of heavy metal,
and four pieces of thin metal were classified as Indeterminate. The majority of the assemblage
was recovered from the 0–40-cmbs levels. The 13 specimens from the lower 40–80 cmbs levels
(vessel glass fragments, a wire nail, the cut nail, six handmade brick fragments) mixed with the
modern plastic bottle cap suggest a disturbed context.
No historic structure is presently located in or near the APE, and no structures were indicated on
the 1951 USGS De Kalb NW topographic map or the 1918 USDA Bowie County soils map.
However, an old standing barn east of the current ROW is likely evidence that this site was
probably a farmstead. The site may extend outside of the APE to the east and west.

Recommendations
Site 41BW768, a late nineteenth- to early twentieth-century artifact scatter situated in a level
portion of the Pleistocene terrace south of a low-lying, periodically inundated area, may extend
outside of the APE. The site yielded a relatively high-density, but unremarkable, artifact
assemblage. The cultural material, in combination with the lack of features, no known historic
structure, and the degree of disturbance, suggests that the portion of the site within the APE has
little research potential. Therefore, no further investigation of the portion of site 41BW768
within the APE is recommended.

77

SITE 41BW769 (WA 578 20 SA001 / CSJ 0085-04-034)

Current Investigations
Site 41BW769 is the fifth site identified and recorded during cultural resources survey in
conjunction with the proposed project to widen an 11.9-mi segment of US 259 from just south of
the Oklahoma/Texas state line to just north of US 82 in De Kalb, Bowie County, Texas. The site
is plotted on the USGS Oak Grove (33094-E6) 7.5' series quadrangle. The APE is defined as the
existing 61-m-wide maximum ROW, which is owned by the state of Texas. Fieldwork for the
cultural resources survey was undertaken in April and June 2010.
Site 41BW769 is a prehistoric site (with minimal historic-era artifacts) situated on the north bank
and within the active floodplain of Mud Creek, which traverses through a gently undulating
prairie landscape (Figure 11). The site is in a state-maintained open prairie setting composed of
dense grasses and forbs. Vegetation adjacent to the site is primarily streamside oak and elm trees
with a moderate understory of ivy and vines. Open prairie grasses surround each quadrant.
Elevation at the site is approximately 358 ft amsl, and the site is located at the southern periphery
of a sequence of prairie mounds. Thenas fine sandy loam represents the majority if not all of the
soils at the site. A typical shovel test revealed 50 cm of yellowish brown (10YR 5/4) fine loose
sand over 30 cm of yellowish brown (10YR 5/6) fine loose sand. US 259 has likely cut the site in
half since materials were found in the northwest and northeast quadrants. The US 259 road
crown has certainly affected any intact sediments immediately adjacent to the highway, and
utility lines running along the east side of US 259 may have disturbed the portion of the site in the
northeast quadrant.
The artifact assemblage from the site was recovered from eight positive shovel tests, with the site
boundary delineated within the APE by six negative shovel tests and the northern edge of Mud
Creek. The site covers a total area of approximately 55 m NW-SE by 57 m E-W. The cultural
assemblage consists of 34 artifacts: 29 lithics (two of which are chipped stone tools), two
prehistoric ceramics, a faunal bone, and two historic-era specimens. The lithics are primarily
debitage (biface thinning [n=8], decortication [n=10], indeterminate medial/distal fragments
[n=3], and shatter [n=6]). Raw material represented among the debitage is undifferentiated chert
(n=19), quartzite (5), siltstone (2), and translucent chert (n=1). Debitage was retrieved from 0–80
cmbs, with the majority (n=23) found from 0–60 cmbs. The two chipped stone tools are an
undifferentiated chert uniface with edge modification from Transect 2/Shovel Test 127 and a
quartzite biface fragment from Transect 2/Shovel Test 128; both tools came from the 40–60-cmbs
level. Also found in the same shovel test and level as the uniface were two untyped prehistoric
ceramic body vessel sherds, as well as a calcined animal long bone shaft. The sherds exhibit a
grit/grog temper and a smoothed exterior; wall thickness of both is 7 mm. The final two artifacts
from the site are a modern plastic (1942–present) machine part (Transect 2/Shovel Test 58; 20–40
cmbs) and an unidentifiable iron fragment (Transect 2/Shovel Test 64; 60–70 cmbs). It may be
noteworthy that the ferrous metal fragment was found 40 cm below debitage in Transect 2/Shovel
Test 64. This occurrence seems to indicate some degree of disturbance, although it is unclear
whether mixing is due to construction activities, or the natural translocation of materials vertically
through the sandy matrix because of the site location in the active floodplain of Mud Creek, or,
conversely, the possibility that the metal was actually in the upper horizons, accidently dislodged
from the sidewall of the shovel test unit and recovered at a lower depth. The last situation seems
most likely because no other historic or modern materials were found below prehistoric materials
78

in any of the other shovel tests. Although no intact cultural features were encountered during
shovel testing, charcoal flecking and a charcoal chunk were observed in three shovel tests
(Transect 1/Shovel Test 43, Transect 1/Shovel Test 58, and Transect 2/Shovel Test 64 from 0–70
cmbs). The piece of plastic machine part was found below the flecking in Transect 1/Shovel Test
58, but no prehistoric material was above it in the unit. In addition to hand-excavated units, two
trenches (BHTs 1 and 2) were excavated to 120 cmbs and 140 cmbs, respectively, but no deeply
buried living surfaces were identified in the profiles of either trench. The site, however, likely
extends beyond the APE to the east and west.

Recommendations
Prehistoric site 41BW769, on the north bank and within the active floodplain of Mud Creek, is in
a gently undulating prairie landscape and likely extends beyond the APE. US 259 has likely cut
the site in half since materials were found in the northwest and northeast quadrants and has
affected any intact sediments immediately adjacent to the highway. Additionally, utility lines
running along the east side of US 259 may have disturbed the portion of the site in the northeast
quadrant. Nonetheless, based on the recovery of chipped stone tools, ceramics, and burned bone,
all from 40–60 cmbs, and the possibility of relatively intact deposits—assuming that the
perceived mixing of the two historic artifacts with the prehistoric material is incidental to the
contextual integrity—the portion of the site within the APE may retain research potential relevant
to Archaic/Woodland/Late Prehistoric settlement and subsistence patterns in the uplands of the
Red River basin. Therefore, additional intensive archeological investigations are recommended
in the portion of 41BW769 within the APE to determine the presence, extent, and integrity of
intact cultural deposits.

SITE 41DT272 (WA 578 19 SA001 / CSJ 0136-03-044)

Current Investigations
Site 41DT272 is one of several sites identified during a 10.7-mi survey along SH 24 in Delta
County, plotted on the 1964 Cooper South (33095-C6) USGS 7.5' series quadrangle, to
investigate the plotted locations of mapped historic structures (MHSs). The existing ROW is
owned by the state of Texas; however, the proposed new ROW will extend onto privately owned
property. The APE for this project consists of the existing TxDOT ROW, which varies from
approximately 19 m to 76 m wide, and proposed new ROW that will be acquired primarily north
of the existing ROW. The new ROW will extend approximately 45 m beyond the existing ROW.
Fieldwork was undertaken in February 2010.
Site 41DT272, the location of MHS #15, consists of the remnants of a historic farmstead situated
within the APE in a privately owned plowed field west of CR 2110 and north of SH 24. The site
is in an upland edge setting on a slightly elevated area just east of the Johns Creek floodplain.
Most of the vegetation within the existing ROW consists of grasses and forbs with primarily
honey mesquite and eastern red cedar trees established along the property fence lines, but also
with some pastureland on private property within the APE. Vegetation along the streams is
primarily hardwoods, particularly oak, elm, mesquite, pecan, bois d‘arc, and locust trees, with a
dense understory of vines, briers, and ivy. The Johns Creek floodplain, in particular, is composed
80

predominantly of dense stands of locust trees. The majority of the existing ROW has been
affected by activities associated with the construction and maintenance of SH 24. A buried gas
line is present within the proposed new ROW north of SH 24 within the Johns Creek floodplain.
According to the BEG (1979), the area in the site vicinity is underlain by the Neylandville
Formation from the Hunt-Delta county line to the floodplain of Johns Creek and by Marlbrook
Marl Formation immediately to the northeast of the Johns Creek floodplain. Site 41DT272 is
plotted on Crockett loam, 1 to 3 percent slopes (NRCS 2010). Shovel Test 39 at site 41DT272
revealed 20 cm of brown clay over 40 cm of a disturbed soil context of gray clay with brownish
yellow and gray mottling.
Site 41DT272 was identified during a visit to the location of MHS #15 (Figure 12). The site
revealed a clearly visible surface scatter of numerous features and artifacts indicative of an early
to mid-twentieth-century historic farmstead. These features included a brick-lined well or cistern
(located outside the APE), a collapsed outhouse, a plow and associated components, and a
barbed-wire fence line with multiple enclosure areas extending from the foundation of a previous
structure into the adjacent Johns Creek floodplain to the west-southwest. Observed artifacts
consisted of several brick fragments, porcelain fragments, and Fiestaware fragments on the
ground surface surrounding the foundation. Since components of the site were clearly visible on
the surface, shovel testing at the site focused on the plowed field surrounding the foundation area
in order to determine if the site extended beyond the surface scatter and if cultivation practices
had destroyed portions of the site. Shovel Tests 20 and 39 were excavated in the plowed field, to
the east and to the north, respectively, of the foundation. Although no cultural materials were
recovered from the shovel tests, fragments of bricks and Fiestaware were observed scattered in
the plowed field, indicating that the contextual integrity of the site has been compromised to some
degree.
Site 41DT272 likely represents remnants of an early to mid-twentieth-century farmstead based on
some of the features observed within the estimated 150-x-30-m area that the site encompasses
within the current APE. The cultural materials observed on the surface at the site consisted of a
brick-lined well or cistern, a collapsed outhouse, gas line fittings, a fence line extending to the
southwest from the location of the structure foundation into the Johns Creek floodplain, as well as
numerous glass jars, gas drums, a porcelain fragment, a plow and associated metal fittings, and a
Fiestaware fragment. According to Huxford and Huxford (1992), the timeframe for Fiestaware
dates to between 1920 and 1950. The remainder of the observed items were also consistent with
the interpretation that 41DT272 represents an early to mid-twentieth-century historic farmstead.
Archival research, including a review of The Texas Historic Overlay (Foster et al. 2006), did not
yield any information regarding previous landowners or site use.
It is highly likely that site 41DT272 may represent one of the many locations of a former tenant
house dating to the early twentieth century. As such, this site could provide information
regarding the subsistence lifestyle of tenant farmers and their participation in the larger national
and world economies. However, similar historic sites have been documented during numerous
investigations at Cooper Lake (see Fields et al. 1997 for site summaries).

81

Figure

12. Plan map of site 41DT272 (CSJ 0136-03-044)

Figure 12. Plan map of site 41DT272 (CSJ 0136-03-044).

Recommendations
Although no cultural materials were recovered from the shovel tests, fragments of bricks and
Fiestaware were observed scattered in the plowed field, indicating that the contextual integrity of
the site has been compromised to some degree. The disturbed nature of the site within the APE
and the low density of subsurface artifacts limit the research potential (it appears that the most
productive area of the evaluated site potentially lies outside the present APE). Based on the
materials recovered from the portion of the site within the APE, it is unlikely that this site would
offer any research potential that has not been fulfilled by previous investigations. Based on the
findings described above, the portion of site 41DT272 within the APE does not meet the criteria
(36 CFR 60.4) for inclusion in the NRHP or the criteria (13 TAC 26.8) for designation as an
SAL. Therefore, no further investigation of the portion of the site within the APE is
recommended.

SITE 41DT273 (WA 578 19 SA001 / CSJ 0136-03-044)

Current Investigations
Site 41DT273 is the second of several sites identified during the 10.7-mi survey along SH 24 in
Delta County to investigate the plotted locations of MHSs. The site is plotted on the 1964
Klondike (33095-C7) USGS 7.5' series quadrangle. The existing ROW is owned by the state of
82

Texas; however, the proposed new ROW will extend onto privately owned property. The APE
for this project consists of the existing TxDOT ROW, which varies from approximately 19.2 m to
76.2 m wide, and proposed new ROW that will be acquired primarily north of the existing ROW.
The new ROW will extend approximately 45.7 m beyond the existing ROW. Fieldwork was
undertaken in February and March 2010.
Site 41DT273, the locations of MHS #5, was recorded as the remnants of a historic-era farmstead
situated within the APE on privately owned land east of McCombs Hill Road and north of SH
24.. The site is in an upland setting west of the Jernigan Creek floodplain and is located on
private property in a maintained area among scattered historic oak trees. The site is
approximately 50 m southwest of a large pond. The BEG (1979) maps the area in the site vicinity
as underlain by the Neylandville Formation. The site is plotted on Crockett loam, 1 to 3 percent
slopes (NRCS 2010). Shovel tests near the foundation revealed approximately 20 cm of very
dark grayish brown to brown silt loam over approximately 20 cm of yellowish to grayish brown
silty clay with mottles. The majority of the existing ROW has been affected by activities
associated with the construction and maintenance of SH 24.
Site 41DT273 was encountered near the recorded location of MHS #5 (Figure 13). Evidence of a
previous structure at the location of MHS #5 was represented by the presence of a modern tool
shed constructed on top of a concrete foundation with a front porch protruding from the base of
the shed. Conversations with the landowners revealed that this particular area has been occupied
for more than 100 years and that the foundation and front porch indeed belonged to a structure
present prior to 1936. Further attesting to the legitimacy of this communication was the presence
of numerous large oak trees lined with historic-age bricks and a stand of several cypress trees
planted over 100 years ago below the dam of a small man-made lake on the property. A shovel
test (Shovel Test 15) was excavated immediately south-southeast of the front porch to search for
further evidence of MHS #5. The shovel test yielded an iron fragment, a charcoal fragment, and a
carbonized deer-sized bone fragment between 0 and 20 cmbs, and a natural clay slip ceramic
fragment between 20 and 40 cmbs. The B horizon, a brown (10YR 5/4) silty clay with red
(2.5YR 5/6) and light gray (10YR 7/1) mottles, was encountered below this lowest zone. Further
personal communication with the landowners revealed that historic artifacts such as those
recovered from Shovel Test 15 have been observed throughout the area surrounding the modern
residence immediately adjacent to MHS #5. This information prompted the placement of a total
of seven additional shovel tests roughly paralleling SH 24 to the west-northwest and eastnortheast of Shovel Test 15 to determine the lateral extent of cultural deposits. Two positive
shovel tests (Shovel Tests 17 and 18) were excavated 10–15 m to the west-northwest of Shovel
Test 15. Historic materials recovered from these units consisted of nails and glass fragments, and
a single interior chert flake found between 0 and 20 cmbs in Shovel Test 17, excavated 15 m
west-northwest of Shovel Test 16. To expedite the delineation process, the spacing of shovel
tests near MHS #5 was expanded to 20 m and 30 m in order to determine the lateral extent of
cultural deposits in the area. Two shovel tests (Shovel Tests 25 and 26) were placed 20 m and
40 m to the west-northwest of Shovel Test 18, respectively, and both returned negative results,
thus delineating the area in this direction. Next, Shovel Tests 27 and 28 were excavated 20 m and
40 m to the east-northeast of Shovel Test 15, respectively. Shovel Test 27 yielded three nails and
two glass fragments between 0 and 15 cmbs, and a B horizon similar to that encountered in
Shovel Test 15 was found directly below this. Shovel Test 28 did not yield any cultural
materials, and the dam of the small man-made lake was approached immediately to the eastnortheast of this unit, thus delineating the area in that direction. Delineation to the north of
Shovel Test 15 was terminated by the presence of the tool shed and the northern boundary of the
83

Figure

13. Plan map of site 41DT273 (CSJ 0136-03-044)

Figure 13. Plan map of site 41DT273 (CSJ 0136-03-044).

APE, and delineation to the south was terminated by disturbances within the existing ROW; the
estimated site area within the APE is approximately 50-x-20 m. The only dateable item
recovered from 41DT273 was a natural clay slip ceramic fragment that falls within the 1875–
1900 time range. This timeframe is consistent with the oral histories provided by the current
residents of the private property where the site was discovered.
The concrete foundation of MHS #5 is still intact and the front porch is clearly visible; however, a
tool shed has recently been constructed on top of the foundation, compromising the integrity of
the site. Surrounding the historic-era foundation at the site are numerous large oak trees that
according to local residents are around 100 years old. The natural clay slip ceramic fragment
from Shovel Test 15 directly adjacent to the front porch also indicates a timeframe of site use
between approximately 1875 and 1900.
As with site 41DT272, site 41DT273 may represent one of the numerous locations of a former
tenant house dating to the early twentieth century. Although this site could provide information
regarding the subsistence lifestyle of tenant farmers and their participation in the larger national
and world economies, similar historic sites have been documented during numerous
investigations at Cooper Lake (see Fields et al. 1997 for site summaries).

Recommendations

SITE 41DT274 (WA 578 19 SA001 / CSJ 0136-03-044)

Current Investigations
Site 41DT274 is the third of several sites identified during the 10.7-mi survey along SH 24,
plotted on the USGS 1964 Klondike (33095-C7) 7.5' series quadrangle, in Delta County to
investigate the plotted locations of MHSs. The existing ROW is owned by the state of Texas;
however, the proposed new ROW will extend onto privately owned property. The APE for this
project consists of the existing TxDOT ROW, which varies from approximately 19 m to 76 m
wide, and proposed new ROW that will be acquired primarily north of the existing ROW. The
new ROW will extend approximately 45 m beyond the existing ROW. Fieldwork was
undertaken in February 2010.
Similar to site 41DT273, which is approximately 475 m to the southwest, 41DT274 is situated
within the APE on privately owned land east of McCombs Hill Road and north of SH24. The site
is in an upland setting west of the Jernigan Creek floodplain and is located on private property
among scattered historic oak trees. The site is approximately 100 m southeast a large pond. The
BEG (1979) maps the area in the site vicinity as underlain by the Neylandville Formation. The
site is plotted on Woodtell loam, 5 to 12 percent slopes (NRCS 2010). Soil profiles in the shovel
tests excavated at 41DT274 were somewhat similar and consisted of very dark brown (10YR 2/2)
and very dark grayish brown (10YR 3/2) loamy topsoils grading into sandy clay B horizons,
although the B horizon in Shovel Test 29 was encountered at 60 cmbs as opposed to 30 and 40
cmbs in Shovel Tests 31 and 30, respectively. The majority of the existing ROW has been
affected by activities associated with the construction and maintenance of SH 24.
A visit to the plotted location of MHS #6 prompted the discovery of site 41DT274, which is a
historic-era site with a minimal prehistoric component (Figure 14). Shovel Test 29 was
excavated approximately 5 m north-northwest of the current ca. 1950 residential structure where
MHS #6 is located. The shovel test yielded burned glass fragments, mirror glass, concrete chunks
(one with the embossed letter ―B‖) between 0 and 20 cmbs; and glass, two chert flakes, and a
quartzite flake between 20 and 40 cmbs. Between 40 and 60 cmbs, a single glass fragment was
recovered. It is unclear whether the mixing of historic and prehistoric artifacts was the result of
glass fragments falling into deeper levels from the sidewall of the upper layers, or if the mixing
was the result of disturbances associated with a buried water line that was within 1–2 m of the
shovel test. Shovel Tests 30 and 45 were excavated 15 m and 30 m east-northeast, respectively,
of Shovel Test 29 but did not yield any cultural materials. These two tests delineated the site to
85

Figure

14. Plan map of site 41DT274 (CSJ 0136-03-044)

Figure 14. Plan map of site 41DT274 (CSJ 0136-03-044).

the east-northeast. Shovel Test 31 was then excavated approximately 25 m to the west of Shovel
Test 29. The interval was expanded there to avoid a storm cellar. The shovel test yielded an
unidentified modern, sawed bone fragment in the upper 20 cmbs. At this point, Shovel Test 31
was near the edge of the northern boundary of the APE, so shovel testing ceased in that direction.
Delineation to the north and south of Shovel Test 29 was prevented by the presence of the
northern APE boundary and by the residence south of Shovel Test 29; the estimated site area
within the APE is approximately 25-x-10 m.
Excavations at site 41DT274 produced two positive shovel test results. Shovel Test 29 yielded a
mixed assemblage of historic and prehistoric artifacts. Glass fragments were found in the same
levels as lithic debitage; however, it is unclear whether the glass was intrusive from the sidewall
of upper levels or if disturbances associated with an adjacent buried water line was responsible
for the mixed assemblage. Either way, no temporally diagnostic materials were found, and the
archeological materials appear to be isolated in nature. Oral history documentation from local
residents of the property where site 41DT274 was discovered indicates that the area has been
utilized for over 100 years, although the names of the previous owners remain unclear.
As with sites 41DT272 and 41DT273, site 41DT274 may represent one of the numerous locations
of a former tenant house dating to the early twentieth century. Although this site could provide
information regarding the subsistence lifestyle of tenant farmers and their participation in the
larger national and world economies, similar historic sites have been documented during
numerous investigations at Cooper Lake (see Fields et al. 1997 for site summaries).

86

Recommendations
The disturbed nature of site 41DT274 within the APE and the low density of subsurface artifacts,
as well as the lack of any temporal artifacts, limit the research potential. Additionally, because of
the location of the positive shovel tests at the northern APE boundary, it is highly possible the
most productive area of the evaluated site potentially lies outside the present APE. Based on the
materials recovered from the portion of the site within the APE, it is unlikely that this site would
offer any research potential that has not been fulfilled by previous investigations. Therefore, the
portion of site 41DT274 within the APE does not meet the criteria (36 CFR 60.4) for inclusion in
the NRHP or the criteria (13 TAC 26.8) for designation as an SAL. Thus, no further investigation
of the portion of the site within the APE is recommended.

SITE 41DT275 (WA 578 19 SA001 / CSJ 0136-03-044)

Current Investigations
Site 41DT275 is the fourth and last of the sites identified during the 10.7-mi survey along SH 24
in Delta County, plotted on the USGS 1964 Klondike (33095-C7) 7.5' series quadrangle, to
investigate the plotted locations of MHSs. The existing ROW is owned by the state of Texas;
however, the proposed new ROW will extend onto privately owned property. The APE for this
project consists of the existing TxDOT ROW, which varies from approximately 19 m to 76 m
wide, and proposed new ROW that will be acquired primarily north of the existing ROW. The
new ROW will extend approximately 45 m beyond the existing ROW. Fieldwork was
undertaken in February 2010.
Site 41DT275 is a historic-era site situated east of Jernigan Creek. The site is within the northern
APE on privately owned land in an upland setting. The area surrounding the site is in a
maintained farmhouse yard. The BEG (1979) maps the area in the site vicinity as underlain by
the Neylandville Formation. The site is plotted on Wilson silty loam 0–2, percent slopes (NRCS
2010). The soil profile from the shovel test revealed 0–35 cmbs of very dark grayish brown silty
clay, over 35–80 cmbs of very dark grayish brown clay with yellowish red mottles. The majority
of the existing ROW has been affected by activities associated with the construction and
maintenance of SH 24.
Investigation at the location of MHS #10 produced a single positive shovel test (Shovel Test 19)
(Figure 15). This test yielded artifacts consisting of an unidentified modern, sawed bone
fragment; a portion of a plastic bag; a white plastic fragment; and a clear plastic fragment with
―New York‖ printed on it. These items were determined to be recent refuse, and no other shovel
tests were excavated near MHS #10. However, the area was assigned the site number 41DT275
due to the presence of a storm cellar and a well or cistern likely associated with the historic
structure mapped on the 1936 General Highway Map of Delta County, Texas. No further work
was performed in this area because the highest probability of finding cultural materials was
farther to the north beyond the northern boundary of the APE.
