Site 41DT275 corresponds to a cistern or well and a storm cellar (within an estimated site area of
approximately 20-x-20 m within the APE), representative of an early to mid-twentieth-century
farmstead likely representing MHS #10. Shovel Test 19 was excavated near the MHS, but only
modern refuse was found in the shovel test between 0 and 20 cmbs. No temporally diagnostic
cultural materials were recovered from 41DT275. Deed history research of the property indicates
that the structure mapped on the 1936 Highway Map of Delta County, Texas was built in 1900
and is currently owned by Anthony Peters; however, no other information was found regarding
the property.
As with sites 41DT272, 41DT273, 41DT274, it is highly likely that site 41DT275 may represent
the location of a former tenant house dating to the early twentieth century, as with many of the
other MHSs along SH 24. As such, these locations could provide information regarding the
subsistence lifestyle of tenant farmers and their participation in the larger national and world
economies. However, similar historic sites have been documented during the numerous
investigations at Cooper Lake (see Fields et al. 1997 for site summaries).

Recommendations
It appears that the most productive area of the evaluated site potentially lies outside the present
APE. The disturbed nature of the site within the APE and the low density of subsurface artifacts
limit the research potential. Based on the modern refuse recovered from the portion of the site
within the APE, it is unlikely that this site would offer any research potential that has not been
88

fulfilled by previous investigations. Therefore, the portion of site 41DT275 within the APE does
not meet the criteria (36 CFR 60.4) for inclusion in the NRHP or the criteria (13 TAC 26.8) for
designation as an SAL. Thus, no further investigation of the portion of the site within the APE is
recommended.

SITES 41HS13 AND 41HS915 (WA 578 1 SA001 / CSJ 0843-07-012)

Previous Investigations
Site 41HS13 was initially recorded in 1962 as a Caddo mortuary site and plotted on the Atlas
west of the Potters Creek Relief channel, immediately north of FM 2625, with the possibility that
part of the site extended into the proposed highway construction APE. Although local relic
hunters had been aware of the site for many years and had looted much of the site prior to its
recording, the site was first recorded as a Protohistoric/historic Caddo mortuary site (Duffield
1962). James Corbin reexamined the site in 1992 during a survey for a gas pipeline and identified
a possibly earlier nonmortuary component consisting of a deposit of lithic flakes, burned rock,
and charcoal flecks. He also speculated that the mortuary component had been largely, if not
completely, destroyed (Corbin 1992b).

Current Investigations
Site 41HS13 was revisited in conjunction with a bridge rehabilitation project at Potters Creek and
Potters Creek Relief along FM 2625. All impacts from the proposed TxDOT project will be
confined to the state-owned existing 44.2-m-wide ROW, which comprises the APE for this
project. The centroid of site 41HS13, on the 1983 Darco, Texas, 7.5' series USGS quadrangle, is
situated in the floodplain west of Potters Creek Relief in Harrison County, approximately 45 m
north of FM 2625. As plotted on the 1992 Atlas site form (Corbin 1992b), the southern boundary
of site 41HS13 likely extends into the northern APE area. Investigators attempted to locate and
evaluate any portion of site 41HS13 within the project APE during survey. At the conclusion of
fieldwork, the excavated cluster of positive shovel tests east of the relief channel and
approximately 145 m east of the plotted location for site 41HS13 was considered to be a separate
site, and this area was assigned the trinomial 41HS915. Geo-Marine, Inc., archeologists
conducted fieldwork on May 7, 2008, and May 27–28, 2008, within the proposed project APE.
According to the present Work Authorization, the Caddo have informed TxDOT that they
consider the area sacred.
Approximately 60 percent of the surveyed area surrounding site 41HS13 is covered with grass,
wildflowers, and blackberry vines. Secondary forest flanks the APE and partially extends into the
ROW to constitute the remaining 40 percent of the major vegetation types. Within the floodplain
is a generalized forest of oak, elm, hickory, sweet gum, and pine on the north side. A moderately
dense understory of poison ivy, Virginia creeper, greenbrier, and other vines occurs within the
tree lines. The underlying geology of the site area consists of Holocene Alluvium within the
floodplain (BEG 1975). The area is mapped as Iuka fine sandy loam, frequently flooded (Golden
et al. 1994). Shovel Test NW 9, approximately 30 m south of the plotted datum for site 41HS13
and just within the northern edge of the APE, revealed 20 cm of brown (10YR 5/3) silty clay
loam, above 30 cm of gray (10YR 6/1) clay with strong brown (7.5YR 5/8) streaks and mottles.
89

Shovel Test SC 4, to the east and south of the highway between the creek and the relief channel
(in the area designated as site 41HS915), revealed dark yellowish brown (10YR 4/6) sandy loam
throughout the excavated depth (0–80 cmbs). The ROW is mostly open grass but it extends
approximately 5 m into the tree lines of the forest along both sides of the roadway. The grasscovered roadbed descends sharply into the floodplain, but the portion of the floodplain between
the two creek channels is slightly higher in elevation than the areas flanking the two channels. A
two-track road extends across the east half of the ROW segment (within 41HS915) between the
two channels north of the highway and east of the plotted location of 41HS13. Shovel testing
along both sides of the roadway indicated that within the floodplain the portions of the ROW
outside and often also along the edge of the tree lines are disturbed.
Shovel testing within the floodplain initially focused on the north side of the highway closest to
the recorded location of 41HS13 (Figure 16). The west side of the relief channel where that site
is plotted could only accommodate two shovel tests spaced 20 m apart on the north side of the
road between standing waters and an access road to a gas pipeline (see Figure 16). One unit was
excavated on the south side of the road. None of the three units yielded cultural material.
The remaining 20 shovel tests in the vicinity of site 41HS13 were placed to the east of the relief
channel: eight on the north side of the road and 12 on the south side. The row of shovel tests
spaced 20 m apart was excavated as far to the north of the ROW as possible, usually less than 5 m
south of the property fence. South of the highway, a row of shovel tests was excavated across the
segment between the two creek channels along the edge of the tree line, and shovel test intervals
were widened to 30 m because of the distance from the recorded site location and the absence of
undisturbed artifact deposits to the north. Despite this presumption, a shovel test yielded an
artifact in a relatively undisturbed context and necessitated a series of delineation shovel tests to
the east, west, and south. Delineation shovel tests were generally spaced in 10-m intervals until
artifacts were no longer encountered.
In total, six artifacts, all chert flakes, were recovered from five positive shovel tests east of Potters
Creek Relief within the floodplain southeast of the plotted location of site 41HS13. The positive
shovel tests were typically inside the tree line (see Figure 16). Four shovel tests within the
floodplain between the two creek channels south of the highway (Shovel Test SC 4 and its
delineation tests, Shovel Tests SC4 S5, SC4 S5 W10, SC4 S5 W20) each produced one chert
flake between 20 and 70 cmbs. One shovel test north of the road (Shovel Test NC 2) produced
two chert flakes, but these were recovered from the upper 10 cm of the unit in a mixed context
with three fragments of thin metal from a tin or can. No additional artifacts were identified in the
other shovel tests excavated south of the plotted position of site 41HS13 west of the channel or to
the southeast between the two channels on the north or south sides of the highway.
Previous investigations identified site 41HS13 on a low rise west of the Potters Creek Relief
channel and north of FM 2625. The chert debitage recovered during the present examination of
the area was consistent with the type of archeological materials observed in the nonmortuary
component of the site by Corbin in 1992. Therefore, the positive shovel tests encountered during
the present occupation were initially interpreted as an extension of 41HS13. It was also
considered possible that the relief channel either did not present a year-round obstacle when the
site was occupied/utilized, or the channel did not exist in prehistory. However, the relief channel
and more than 100 m, including 12 negative shovel tests, separated the positive shovel tests
encountered during the present investigation from the documented site location. Thus, TxDOT
revised this same-site interpretation to conclude that the more recently excavated cluster of shovel

90

tests east of the relief channel is a separate site. The new site has been assigned the trinomial
41HS915, with an estimated site area within the APE of approximately 75-x-25 m, stretching
northeast–southwest across FM 2625.
The materials recovered at 41HS915 from the north side of the highway are clearly in a disturbed
context, and those to the south are sparse, lack diagnostic artifacts, and offer little research
potential. The deepest buried artifact was recovered in Shovel Test SC 4 S5 at 70 cmbs. The unit
was excavated to 100 cmbs, but no other artifacts were recovered. The other undisturbed positive
shovel tests were sterile below 60 cmbs, and all were excavated to 80 cmbs. The potential exists
for undisturbed deposits within the privately owned properties adjacent to the ROW where
artifacts were identified during the present investigation; however, this possibility cannot be
explored at present because the material is outside the limits of the present project and not subject
to the National Historic Preservation Act or the Antiquities Code of Texas.

Recommendations
Shovel test results showed that construction of the bridges and present highway alignment has
disturbed part of 41HS915. Artifacts were recovered between the two creek channels on both
sides of the highway, although those encountered to the north were in a disturbed context. A
narrow (approximately 5 m) strip of undisturbed soils south of the highway yielded artifacts, but
these were few in number and limited to debitage, thus offering little research potential. The
potential exists for undisturbed deposits within the privately owned properties adjacent to the
ROW where artifacts were identified during the present investigation, but at present, this area
could not be assessed because it was outside the limits of the present project. The portion of
41HS915 within the APE does not meet the criteria as enumerated under 36 CFR 60.4 and 13
TAC 26.8 to qualify for inclusion in the NRHP or for designation as an SAL and is recommended
not eligible for inclusion in the NRHP or for designation as an SAL. Thus, no further
archeological work will be necessary as per 36 CFR 800.5(b) and 13 TAC 26.2 within the
currently investigated portion of site 41HS915.
No indication of material associated with site 41HS13 was found within the current APE.
Nonetheless, should impacts extend beyond the currently investigated ROW, monitoring of the
site 41HS13 area is recommended.

SITE 41HS559 (WA 578 20 SA001 / CSJ 0843-02-012)

Previous Investigations
According to the Atlas, prehistoric site 41HS559 was recorded at the northern end of Little
Cypress Bayou on a low rise above the floodplain by local avocational archeologist Marshall
McIntosh in 1994, and information regarding the site is restricted to his surface observations. As
recorded, the site was a surface scatter of lithic artifacts (dart and arrow projectile points, lithic
flaking debris) and pottery sherds located partially within the FM 450 proposed ROW. The
scatter of artifactual materials was observed in association with the southern dam of a large fish
hatchery near the northern end of Little Cypress Bayou. The recorder suspected that the majority
of the site had been disturbed by construction of the dam, but he suggested that an intact portion
of the site might remain within the proposed ROW APE.
92

Current Investigations
Site 41HS559 was partially investigated—only within the state-owned ROW—during the current
cultural resources survey for the proposed project to realign FM 450 and replace the bridge at
Little Cypress Creek, which is in the center of the Little Cypress Creek valley, in Harrison
County. Site 41HS559 is on the Harleton (32094-F5) USGS 7.5' series topographic quadrangle.
The APE for this project is the existing state-owned ROW, approximately 24.4 m wide from the
centerline of FM 450, but additional proposed ROW will be acquired from private landowners
west of FM 450. The site center is plotted west of the proposed ROW, but the site extends into
the project APE within both the proposed ROW and state-owned land. At the time of survey,
access to the proposed ROW on private property had not been granted, and the site was in an
inundated area. Fieldwork was undertaken March 29–31, 2010.
Site 41HS559 is situated near the northern end of Little Cypress Bayou, on the southern side of a
dam that is part of a large fish hatchery. The overstory along Little Cypress Bayou consists of
pine, oak, and sparse juniper trees. Dense hydrophilic understory vegetation exists over the
majority of the bayou. The site is in the hardwood forest, which itself is within the inundated
bayou. Both the main channel and an intermittent cutoff channel of Little Cypress Creek intersect
the project area near the tight bend in FM 450 approximately 400 m south of the site location.
The underlying geology of the Little Cypress Creek valley is mapped as undifferentiated
Quaternary Alluvium (BEG 1975), and the soil in the site area is mapped as Socagee silty clay
loam, frequently flooded. Shovel Test 20, which is one of the closest shovel tests to the site plot
and excavated to 50 cmbs, revealed 5 cm of very dark gray (10YR 3/1) root mat, over 15 cm of
yellowish brown (10YR 5/4) sandy loam, over 30 cm of dark grayish brown (10YR 4/2) sandy
loam, saturated, gley; water was encountered at 50 cmbs.
An examination of USGS topographic maps indicates that the fish hatchery was constructed
between 1961 and 1978. There do not appear to have been additional significant land
modifications to the area since its construction. The current ROW was found to be moderately
disturbed from a road berm and ditch. All shovel tests within the current ROW were placed at the
edge of the ROW where disturbance was thought to be the most minimal. Shovel tests were
approximately 30 cm in diameter with a planned excavated depth of 80 cmbs or until bedrock or
water was encountered. Shovel test fill was screened through 0.25-inch hardware cloth, and data
about the soils and shovel test contents were recorded on a standardized form. Shovel test
locations were recorded with a handheld GPS unit. During survey current, the ROW was found
to be moderately disturbed from a road berm and ditch, and soils were found to be extremely
saturated; within the immediate area of site 41HS559, the ground was completely inundated
outside of the current ROW.
Due to the proximity of site 41HS559 to the fish hatchery, the northern portion of Little Cypress
Bayou was shovel-tested at 30-m intervals (Figure 17). Shovel Tests 4–20 were all excavated
within the current ROW on both sides of the road near the hatchery and the plotted location of
site 41HS559. Standing water in many shovel tests prevented the practicality of excavating many
of the shovel tests to 80 cmbs. No cultural materials were found in any of the shovel tests.
Nonetheless, the possibility remains that cultural materials from the site exist within the proposed
ROW, which was not investigated because of lack of access to private property.

93

Recommendations
Based on the current observations and previous findings, additional investigation is needed in the
plotted site area within the privately owned proposed ROW when access is obtained and during a
drier season.

SITE 41TT108 (WA 578 1 SA001 / CSJ 3404-01-011)

Previous Investigations
Milton Bell of the former Texas State Department of Highways and Public Transportation
(SDHPT), now TxDOT, recorded this site in 1978 as an open multicomponent prehistoric
campsite (75-x-90 m) and collected petrified wood, chipped stone flakes, and bone from the
surface (Bell 1978). SDHPT tested the site in 1980. At that time, the site was considered to
consist of two Archaic components and two Caddo components, including a pre-bow-and-arrow
Caddo I occupation (Young 1981), although more recent interpretations would regard the site as
consisting of Late Archaic, Woodland, and Formative/Early Caddo components. Recovered
materials included chipped stone tools and cores, debitage, ground stone tools, burned rock,
pottery, faunal materials, and one human burial (Young 1981). The Atlas indicates that site
41TT108 is a designated SAL.

Current Investigations
Site 41TT108 is plotted just north of state-owned existing highway ROW for AM 3417 in Titus
County on the 1968 (1976) Harvard, Texas, 7.5' series USGS quadrangle. The APE for the
TxDOT bridge rehabilitation project on FM 3417 at Tankersley Creek varies from approximately
49 m to 76 m. Because the southern boundary of the site likely extends into the northern APE
area, investigators attempted to locate and evaluate any portion of the site within the project APE
during survey. The current fieldwork was conducted in May 2008.
As plotted on the Atlas, site 41TT108 extends into or is adjacent to the project APE. The site is
plotted west of Tankersley Creek and north of FM 3417 on the toe of a low, gently sloping terrace
overlooking the floodplain of the creek. Mixed hardwoods and brush line the creek and its
channels to the east. The site, as plotted west of the channel, is at the edge of a forested area of
mixed hardwoods and brush, but vegetation within the ROW to the south of the plotted location is
almost entirely knee high grasses and wildflowers. Elevated areas occur inside the ROW, and
although the slopes of these elevations appear to have been mechanically altered by construction
of the present highway alignment, the tops appear to be largely undisturbed.
The underlying geology of the area is Holocene Alluvium within the floodplain, which is flanked
to the west by the Eocene period Wilcox Group undivided (BEG 1979). Soils in the area consist
primarily of Wolfpen loamy fine sand, 2 to 5 percent slopes (Roberts 1990:103). The profile of
Shovel Test NW 6, near the site center, revealed yellowish brown (10YR 5/4) sandy loam to 40
cmbs, over 20 cm of light yellowish brown (10YR 6/4), over 20 cm of yellowish brown (10YR
5/4) sandy loam, with mottled clay near 80 cmbs. The roadbed is elevated, and ground
disturbance was evident in the open area outside the APE, west of a wooded property.
95

Nineteen shovel tests were placed west of Tankersley Creek on the north side of the highway
closest to the plotted location of site 41TT108 (Figure 18). A single transect of east-to-west
shovel tests was excavated along the visibly undisturbed portion of the landform, typically 1–5 m
south of the property fence. Shovel test intervals, initially to be spaced at 30 m, were reduced to
10 m when stone flakes were encountered in Shovel Test NW 2 in the east. Two delineation
shovel tests spaced at 10-m intervals were also excavated to the east. Intervals to the west of
Shovel Test NW7 were increased to 20 m after several consecutive positive shovel tests
suggested that the site would extend a great distance farther. Shovel testing to the west
terminated after three consecutive sterile shovel tests and the landform began to descend toward a
drainage culvert.
Seven shovel tests yielded cultural materials, indicating that site 41TT108 extends into the
existing highway ROW. All of these materials were recovered from public property inside the
state-owned highway ROW, and all were collected. Lithic debitage constituted the primary class
(76 percent) of the 25 artifacts recovered. Of these 19 flakes, 16 were quartzite, two were chert,
and one was hematite. Burned sandstone (n=5) was the second most common artifact class (20
percent), and a piece of petrified wood of undetermined function constituted the remaining 4
percent of the recovered artifacts. Artifact density averaged 3.6 objects per positive shovel test;
Shovel Test NW 3 and Shovel Test NW 8 contained the highest number of recovered materials
(n=6 each). Artifacts were recovered from depths of 0–60 cmbs with most (n=13) occurring
between 40 and 60 cmbs. All archeological materials were confined to the knoll, which
demonstrated two high areas. Interestingly, the two shovel tests excavated between these high
spots were sterile. Four shovel tests excavated along the south slope of the knoll contained
disturbed soils mixed with modern materials. Fragments of modern bottle glass were recovered
from the two shovel tests excavated south of Shovel Test NW 2, and a piece of plastic, probably
from a drinking tumbler, was observed in the fill of Shovel Test 3 S5. Chunks of asphalt were
present in the bottom of Shovel Test NW 3 S10.
The south slope of the knoll in the northwest quadrant demonstrated compromised integrity
because of artificial contouring during road construction, and shovel tests south of Shovel Test
NW 2 and Shovel Test NW 3 contained mixed soils and modern trash, indicating disturbance in
this area. However, typically 10 m of the landform south of the property fence along the top of
the landform demonstrated intact natural soils that frequently exceeded 50 cm in depth, and this
area indicated contextual integrity. Shovel tests along the knoll on the west side of the private
road indicated sandy loam deposits that ranged from 10–45 cmbs before clay subsoil was
encountered. The thicker deposits were farther to the west and suggest that possibly construction
of the current FM 3417 alignment removed significant portions of the original surface soils.

Recommendations
Site 41TT108 is a designated SAL. Whether disturbance to the portion of the site that extends
into the current project area will affect the site‘s status is presently unclear. Artifact
concentration was low to moderate within the positive shovel tests, and diagnostic materials were
not recovered. However, several shovel tests spanning roughly 100 linear m contained artifacts,
and over half the recovered artifacts were concentrated at depths greater than 40 cmbs, therefore
suggesting intact deposits cover an extensive area. Aside from modern trash, only lithic materials
were recovered and might indicate the Archaic component of the site‘s occupation.
Archeological materials appeared to be restricted within the ROW to an undisturbed 10-m-wide
96

strip along the top of the knoll north of the highway west of the creek. In the event that proposed
construction extends north of the presently disturbed portions of the ROW, further evaluation of
this strip should be undertaken to determine whether it contributes to the overall significance of
site 41TT108 as enumerated under 34 CFR 60.4 and 26 TAC 26.8 and whether any impacts to it
will affect the overall status of the site pursuant to 36 CFR 800.5(a)(1) and 26 TAC 26.8. If it is
determined that the area will not be affected or can be avoided, no further archeological work will
be necessary as per 36 CFR 800.5(b).

98

CHAPTER 6
CONCLUSIONS AND RECOMMENDATIONS

RESULTS
Fifteen work authorizations were issued under TxDOT Contract No. 578 xx SA001 (see Table 2)
for two impact evaluations and 33 archeological surveys (with two CSJs involving additional
field work, making 35 total field projects); the final work authorization included the production of
this report. The 35 field projects were conducted in the Atlanta (n=22), Dallas (n=3), and Paris
(n=10) districts. The two impact evaluations were for bridge replacements. The archeological
surveys were in conjunction with the following transportation activities:
3 bridge rehabilitations
22 bridge replacements (two of which were impact evaluations; and two with additional test
excavations or trenching)
1 bridge replacement with road widening
1 pavement reconstruction with an associated impact evaluation
1 road realignment with bridge replacement
4 road widenings (one with scraping along outside of cemetery fence)
1 for construction of temporary haul roads and drill pads associated with bridge construction
2 post-review discovery investigations
The fieldwork for these investigations in the three TxDOT districts was undertaken under Texas
Antiquities Permit Number 4815. Individual interim reports were coordinated with the THC
through TxDOT Environmental Affairs Division. Artifact collections taken from state property
and from private property with the landowner‘s consent as well as all associated notes and
materials are curated at TARL, University of Texas at Austin.
Fourteen sites were revisited or newly recorded during this contract. Preliminary archival
research revealed four previously recorded sites plotted within or extending into highway ROWs
within proposed project areas. Two of these sites (41BW166 and 41TT108) were found to extend
into the project APEs. Of the two other sites, no evidence of 41HS13 was found within the APE
because the site did not extend into the ROW, and access to the plotted location of 41HS559 in
the proposed ROW was not granted by the landowner (only the small portion of the site within
the existing state-owned ROW was investigated). The remaining 10 sites—41BW761,
99

41BW766, 41BW767, 41BW768, 41BW769, 41DT272, 41DT273, 41DT274, 41DT275, and
41HS915—were newly recorded during this contract.
Among the 14 sites or portions of sites identified within the project APEs, four (41BW761,
41BW766, 41HS13, and 41HS559) are prehistoric, and three (41BW768, 41DT272, and
41DT275) are historic-era occupations. Six sites yielded both prehistoric and historic remains—
predominantly prehistoric (sites 41BW166, 41BW769, 41HS915, and 41TT108) with very
minimal (one to three) pieces of historic material; and predominantly historic (41BW767,
41DT273, and 41DT274) with limited (one to three) prehistoric artifacts.
Ten of the sites yielded solely subsurface material (41BW166, 41BW761, 41BW766, 41BW767,
41BW768, 41BW769, 41DT273, 41DT274, 41HS915, and 41TT108). Site 41DT272 exhibited
only a historic surface scatter with structural features, and site 41DT275 was identified merely by
structural remains. Site 41HS13 yielded no cultural materials within the surveyed APE but was
originally recorded as a lithic scatter with FCR and charcoal flecks. The landowner did not grant
full access to the plotted location of site 41HS559, which was originally recorded as a surface
scatter of debitage and pottery sherds.
All of these sites likely extend beyond the investigated APEs, but those areas beyond the APEs
were not inspected. Therefore, only the portions of the sites within the APEs can be evaluated for
NRHP and SAL eligibility, and thus, recommendations apply only to the site portions within the
APEs. Further investigations are recommended to determine the presence, extent, and integrity of
intact cultural deposits for the portions of prehistoric sites 41BW166 and 41BW769 within the
APE, because these sites may contain intact features, a relative degree of contextual integrity, or
varied artifact classes and retain research potential. Although no additional investigation of site
41HS13 within the APE is recommended, this site should be monitored if future impacts extend
beyond the currently investigated ROW. Site 41HS559 is partially on property to which access
was not granted, and therefore, since only the site area on state-owned ROW was inspected,
further investigation of this site will be necessary when full access is obtained. In the event that
proposed construction extends north of the presently disturbed and investigated portions of the
ROW at site 41TT108 (a designated SAL), further evaluation of this strip should be undertaken to
determine whether it contributes to the overall significance of the site. Of the remaining sites, the
portions of sites 41BW761, 41BW766, 41BW767, 41BW768, 41DT272, 41DT273, 41DT274,
41DT275, and 41HS915 within the APE do not meet the criteria (36 CFR 60.4) for inclusion in
the NRHP or the criteria (13 TAC 26.8) for designation as SALs, and no further research is
warranted for the portion of these sites within the APE.

EVALUATION AND DISCUSSION OF STRATEGIES
In most cases, impact evaluations are conducted prior to a full archeological survey involving
shovel testing, mechanical trenching, or a combination of both. If observations made during the
evaluation effort reveal extensive natural and man-made disturbances that preclude the presence
of archeological historic properties or state archeological landmarks and thus obviate the
necessity for an intensive survey, the impact evaluation is often the only archeological field
assessment that many project areas undergo. Conversely, when survey is warranted, gathering
information based on impact evaluations expedites intensive survey planning through project
area-specific information, even when large tracts of new ROW necessitate mechanical trenching.
The location of utilities, the number and placement of trenches, soil and geological contexts, and
100

access restrictions—all information germane to the survey—can be predetermined with an impact
evaluation. For impact evaluations to be effective, however, researchers must have specific
project descriptions, scaled drawings, and right-of-entry to any new ROW, which, unfortunately,
is not the case in some project areas. However, for proposed project areas where access has been
denied, the impact evaluation can still be a valuable tool in determining whether or not an
intensive survey is warranted for that property through observations taken from adjacent areas.
An archeological survey usually follows an impact evaluation. Information from the impact
evaluation often results in intensive archeological survey of only short segments of a proposed
highway or road project where there is potential for, or existence of, archeological deposits or
sites rather than an intensive survey of the entire project APE. As with impact evaluations,
specific project descriptions and scaled drawings as well as right-of-entry are necessary,
particularly when conducting investigations in unassessed areas of proposed APEs outside of
preexisting ROWs. All shovel testing and trenching projects are conducted in accord with the
current Council of Texas Archeologists guidelines as approved by THC, and archeological sites
are normally found at this level of investigation. Sites can be assessed and recommendations
made for either no further work or for testing to determine NRHP eligibility and/or SAL
designation.
The survey efforts summarized in this report reinforce two aspects of archeological properties
within existing highway ROWs: (1) the previous disturbance related to road construction, utility
trenches, and maintenance often destroys the contextual integrity of site contexts; and (2) the
plotting of sites recorded long ago is often inaccurate. Of the portions of the 14 sites present with
the proposed APE of these road improvement projects, only five are considered to be potentially
eligible for inclusion in the NRHP or for designation as an SAL. The remaining nine sites either
lack contextual integrity due to the many impacts related to road construction or utility
placements, or because the portion of the site within the APE exhibits limited research potential.
The survey of 38.2 miles of ROW or 1,385.6 acres of APE, however, did reveal statistics that
appear to be the norm for northeast Texas. For example, one archeological site was found for
every 98.97 acres surveyed. One archeological site per 100 acres has been the presumed density
for large tract surveys in the area for years. Furthermore, the sites were found in only seven of
the 33 survey projects or in only 21.2 percent of the project areas. This figure is very close to the
23 percent figure that Peter calculated for 437 project areas examined under contracts 570 xx
PD002 (Wurtz et al. 2003), 570 xx PD003 (Peukert et al. 2003), 572 xx SA012 (Largent et al.
2005), 574 xx SA004 (Phillips et al. 2007), and 575 xx SA010 (Allday and Bastis 2008). The
limited impact area of many of the projects and the presence of many of the projects on low-order
streams contribute to this low percentage of project areas containing archeological sites.

RECOMMENDATIONS
Several recommendations can be made to promote a more consistent return of the necessary
information from the field. Some recommendations reiterate similar recommendations offered in
previous summary reports (i.e., Allday and Bastis 2008; Largent et al. 2005; Peukert et al. 2003;
Phillips et al. 2007; and Wurtz et al. 2003), but they bear repeating:
1. Gathering available map and archival information prior to fieldwork and a subsequent
recommendation is essential for a cost-effective approach. Many of the work
authorizations under this contract involved an APE with previously recorded sites that
101

needed to be revisited to determine their existence/condition/eligibility status. Thus,
literature and Sites Atlas research related to site locations is imperative for gathering
known data and identifying plotted locations of previously known sites. Six new historicera sites or major site components were recorded during the current contract. To
facilitate location and identification of potential historic-era sites and structures within
the ROW or APE during fieldwork, a review of The Texas Historic Overlay (Foster et al.
2006) and a review of both current and early editions of topographic, soils, and county
road maps prior to undertaking an impact evaluation or archeological survey remains
essential. Examination of historic aerial photographs of APEs (when available) prior to
survey efforts also helps focus the field investigations.
In instances where historic-era cultural remains are encountered, additional
resources/archives may also warrant careful examination. The continued reliance on
deed/title searches, however, is unwarranted. Deed/title searches only rarely provide the
level of detail that one is looking for to aid in the determination of eligibility for inclusion
in the NRHP or for designation as an SAL. Interviews with local informants/landowners
often provide more pertinent data.
2. Impact evaluations remain an essential tool for identifying areas for survey, trenching, or
both, as well as for clearing specific areas for additional construction projects. Only two
work authorizations under this contract were issued for impact evaluations solely. It is
our understanding that the impact evaluation can be a part of any survey effort; however,
we feel that there has been a change of focus to conducting intensive survey only. We
feel that the impact evaluation is a valuable first step in focusing intensive survey efforts
on those areas most likely to contain sites. We understand that interested parties may not
favor such an approach, but when investigations are finding sites in only 21 percent of the
project areas, it makes sense to focus survey efforts within a subset of the total project
areas.
3. Survey remains essential in locating sites within project areas. However, survey
conditions were less than ideal for the 2010 spring and early summer season within the
project areas. Heavy rains and significant surface water in northeast Texas precluded
survey within many portions of the projects or caused numerous trips to the project areas.
Geo-Marine personnel persevered in surveying areas when conditions were certainly less
than ideal in order to meet tight deadlines near the end of this contract as specified by
TxDOT personnel. In most cases, the short-term work periods were essential for TxDOT
to meet projected bidding and construction letting dates.
Clear and regular
communications should be maintained between investigators and TxDOT personnel
concerning the timeframe and full scope of what is required in order to conduct adequate
survey and meet TxDOT changes in required deadlines.
4. Experience with mechanical trenching during this contract revealed valuable lessons
concerning examination of trench walls and what can be interpreted concerning the
presence or absence of cultural material. In certain soil conditions, for example in a
fluvial setting or in homogeneous soils where stratigraphic layers may not be evident,
cultural materials of the same color as, or hard to identify in, the soil matrix may not
always be observable when troweling the trench walls. Subsequent excavation of shovel
tests in the same area sometimes reveals cultural deposits. Consequently, mechanical
trenching, in some environmental settings, is only suited for identifying living surfaces
102

and is often only informative concerning the presence or absence of cultural material if
accompanied by screening a sample of the removed soil matrix or the excavation of
additional units, larger than a shovel test, adjacent to the trench.

APPENDIX A
GLOSSARY OF TECHNICAL TERMS

Adze: Tool whose cutting edge is at a right angle to the handle and that is used in working wood.
Area of potential effects (APE): ―Area of potential effects means the geographic area or areas
within which an undertaking may directly or indirectly cause changes in the character or use of
historic properties, if any such properties exist. The area of potential effects is influenced by the
scale and nature of an undertaking and may be different for different kinds of effects caused by
the undertaking‖ [36 CFR Part 800.16(d)]. Essentially, the area of potential effects (APE) is the
area of reasonably anticipated project impacts and is actually three-dimensional, consisting of
length, width, and depth.
Arrow point: Point used to tip an arrow, which is propelled by a bow.
Biface: Piece of conchoidally fracturing stone that has had flakes removed from both faces to
create a tool.
Dart point: Point used to tip a throwing spear or dart, which is propelled by an atlatl.
Debitage: Debris generated by the removal through percussion/pressure of flakes, chips, and
chunks to make stone tools.
Eligible for Inclusion in the National Register: ―The term eligible for inclusion in the National
Register includes both properties formally determined as such in accordance with regulations of
the Secretary of the Interior and all other properties that meet the National Register criteria‖ [36
CFR 800.16(l)(2)].
FCR (fire-cracked rock): Rock altered by extreme heat; used in rock-lined hearths or middens, as
a long-lasting heat-source, or as boiling stones dropped in liquids in skin or pottery containers.
Fill section: Introduced fill used to elevate the approaches to a bridge above the surrounding
terrain.
Flake: Generally thin piece of conchoidally fractured stone with a positive bulb of percussion
showing that it was removed from the parent piece by percussion/pressure.
Grog: Crushed fired clay added as temper to clay used in making ceramic vessels.
Historic property: ―Historic property means any prehistoric or historic district, site, building,
structure, or object included in, or eligible for inclusion in, the National Register of Historic
Places maintained by the Secretary of the Interior. This term includes artifacts, records, and
remains that are related to and located within such properties. The term includes properties of
traditional religious and cultural importance to an Indian tribe or Native Hawaiian organization
and that meet the National Register criteria‖ [36 CFR 800.16(l)(1)]. Essentially, a historic
property is a cultural resource included in, or eligible for inclusion in, the National Register of
Historic Places.
Impact Evaluation: Onsite inspection as the initial step in determining whether subsequent
survey of a particular area is warranted, given the anticipated impacts of the project, the existing
level of disturbance, and the likelihood of archeological deposits in good context for a proposed
Transportation Activity.
A-3

Megafauna: Particularly large animal such as bison and mammoth, usually associated with the
Pleistocene era and extinct by the beginning of the Holocene era.
Midden: Accumulation of occupational debris, particularly organic remains.
National Register of Historic Places (NRHP) Eligibility Criteria: The Advisory Council on
Historic Preservation has set forth the following regulations to define significance and to
determine eligibility of cultural resources:
The quality of significance in American history, architecture, archeology, engineering, and culture is
present in districts, sites, buildings, structures, and objects that possess integrity of location, design,
setting, material, workmanship, feeling, and association and
(a) that are associated with events that have made a significant contribution to the broad patterns of
our history; or
(b) that are associated with the lives of persons significant in our past; or
(c) that embody the distinctive characteristics of a type, period, or method of construction, or that
represent the work of a master, or that possess high artistic values, or that represent a significant
and distinguishable entity whose components may lack individual distinction; or
(d) that have yielded or may be likely to yield, information important in prehistory or history [39 CFR
Part 60].

Post-review discovery survey: Survey consisting of a site visit to rapidly evaluate potential
historic properties discovered during construction
Projectile point: Inclusive term for arrow and dart points.
Scraper: Tool with generally thick, unifacially modified edges used to work hides, bone, and
wood.
Sherd: A piece of broken pottery.
State Archeological Landmark (SAL): The Texas Historical Commission ―uses one or more of
the following criteria when assessing the appropriateness of official landmark designation, and/or
the need for further investigations under the permit process:
(1) the site has the potential to contribute to a better understanding of the prehistory and/or
history of Texas by the addition of new and important information;
(2) the site‘s archeological deposits and the artifacts within the site are preserved and
intact, thereby supporting the research potential or preservation interests of the site;
(3) the site possesses unique or rare attributes concerning Texas prehistory and/or history;
(4) the study of the site offers the opportunity to test theories and methods of preservation,
thereby contributing to new scientific knowledge;
(5) the high likelihood that vandalism and relic collecting has occurred or could occur, and
official landmark designation is needed to insure [sic] maximum legal protection, or
alternatively further investigations are needed to mitigate the effects of vandalism and
relic collecting when the site cannot be protected‖ [Texas Administrative Code (13
TAC Part 2.§26.8)]. Essentially, a state archeological landmark (SAL) is the state
equivalent of a historic property.
Survey: Fieldwork to locate archeological remains within the project area for a proposed
Transportation Activity, including on-foot examination of the surface, shovel testing, and
subsurface mechanical trenching (where appropriate) within the area of potential effects.
A-4

Temper: Nonplastic materials added to clay to decrease the risk of cracking when firing ceramic
vessels.
Transportation Activity:
Any proposed project involving the development, design,
construction, or maintenance of the state‘s intermodal transportation system.

A-5
